This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission in June
1991, composed of Messrs. James Adams (Division Chief, LA4HR), Luis Pisani
(Mission Leader, LA4HR), and Juan Prawda (Education Specialist, LATHR). The
project preparation team included Manuel Zymelman (Sr. Economist, APTED),
Robert McMeekin (Human Resources Economist, LA4HR), Donald Holsinger
(Education Specialist, PHREE), Marcos Sugar (Disbursement Officer, LOAEL), and
Ms. Marta Molares (Sr. Counsel, LEGLA) of the Bank; and Consultants to the
Bank:  Messrs. Ernesto Schiefelbein (Economist, UNESCO), Manfredo Linder
(Procurement Specialist), and Ms. Anita Artaza (Operational Assistant).
Messrs. James W. Adams and Ping-Cheung Loh are the managing Division Chief and
the Department Director, respectively, for the operations. Peer reviewers for
the project were Mr. Manuel Zymelman (Sr. Economist, AFTED), Mesdames. Himelda
Martinez (Sr. Education Specialist, ASTPH) and Eleanor Schreiber (Sr.
Operational Officer, LA3HR); Ms. Lien-Hiep Nguyen (Staff Assistant, LA4HR)
provided assistance in the production of the report. This report was edited
by Mr. William Mayville (Editor, Consultant, LA4DR).

REPUBLIC OF CHILE
PRIMARY EDUCATION IMPROVEMENT PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Loan and Proiect Summarv
Borrower:                Republic of Chile
Beneficiarv:             Ministry of Education (MINEDUC)
Amount:                  US$170 million, equiv4.ent
Terms:                   Annuity with 17 years maturity and a 4-year grace
period, with interest at the Bank's standard variable
rate.
Prolect Obiectives:      The central project objective is to assist the Govern-
ment in enhancing the efficiency, quality, and equity of primary
education in selected schools in urban and rural areas. The project
would also:
(a) expand the coverage and enhance the quality of preschool
education to increase primary school preparedness and reduce late
entry, repetition, and dropout in primary schools, especially for
5-year-olds living in poverty conditions;
(b) strengthen the institutional capacity of the MINEDUC central,
regional, and provincial offices, as well aa that of the education
departments of the munic'palities to direct and manage a
decentralized education system, and improve the managerial sk'llc of
the municipal and private-subsidized preschool and primary school
administrators? and
(c) assess alternative cost-effective approaches to meet the
secondary education needs of primary education graduates.
Protect Descri,tion:    The project would support the above objectives by
financing four components:
Primary Education (74 percent of total project cost): (i) imple-
menting 5,000 locally based quality improvement projects of a
pedagogical decentralization program (PRODEPE) through the
participation of teachers and principals of 5,000 selected municipal
and private-subsidized primary schools; (ii) strengthening the
educational testing system to assess and monitor student academic
achievement for about 240,000 students from either fourth or eighth
grades every year; (iii) providing inservice training for about
78,000 teachers, 8,000 school principals, and 625 supervisors;
(iv) distributing, on a targeted cost-recovery scheme, about 24.5
million primary textbooks for all municipal and private-subsidized
primary students, about 870,000 sets of complementary reading and
teaching materials, and teaching equipment for primary schools;
(v) hiring 500 new teachers to provide eight grades in 500 incom-
plete rural primary schools; (vi) constructing 500 classrooms in
rural areas; (vii) upgrading and refurbishing classrooms in about
2,500, mostly rural, primary schools; (viii) establishing an



- viii -
experimental education communication computer network in 66, mostly
rural, primary schools; (ix) providing henlth screening and
referrals to 250,000 first graders every year; and (x) establishing
monetary incentives for inservice teacher training and lecturing in
deprived, mostly rural primary schools.
Preschool Education (16 percent of total proiect cost)s (i) expand-
ing the coverage of preschool education for about 16,000 urban and
30,000 rural, mostly 5-year-old children of low-income urban and
rural families by hiring 23 supervisors, 140 teachers, and 1,255
paraprofessionals; providing about 3,000 workshops to train 23,400
rural parents in the early stimulation of their children; construct-
ing 100 classrooms and refurbishing 75 rural facilities donated by
the communities; providing 18,000 daily food rations and 40,300 sets
of didactic materials; and (ii) enhancing the quality of existing
preschool services by hiring 50 supervisors, 163 teachers, and 164
paraprofessionals; providing inservice teacher training courses for
about 200 supervisors, 3,300 preschool principals and teachers, and
2,700 paraprofessionals; designing, producing, and distributing
about 102,000 sets of teaching materials; strengthening parent
participation through training workshops and a massive media
campaign; and establishing evaluation studies to ascertain the cost-
effecti.rnass of each preschool alternative implemented and the
medium-term impact of preschool on the child's education, behavior,
and skills.
Institutional Development (7 nercent of total proiect cost):
(i) reinforcing managerial skills at the central MINEDUC, regional,
and provincial levels by training about 500 staff to improve their
planning, programming, budgeting, and monitoring capacities;
(ii) providing management training to about 670 municipal education-
al employees (from 280 municipal departments of education, and 54
municipal Corporaciones); (iii) providing management skill training
for about 1,200 preschool and primary school administrators; and
(iv) developing practical manuals for planning, programming,
administration, budgeting, and monitoring decentralized education
activities; and (v) establishing an educational information system;
and
Secoadarv Education (1 oercent of total 2roiect cost): financing
about 12 studies on curriculum development, teacher training and
evaluation, as well as external and internal efficiency of secondary
education to assess cost-effective secondary education strategies te
meet the future needs of greater numbers of primary education
graduates.
Expected Benefits:       The principal benefits include:
(a) enhancing the efficiency and quality of preschool and primary
education through improved educational inputs;
(b) improving the equity of the education system, mainly through
targeted preschool expansion, relevant teaching materials and
infrastructure for the rural areas, and inistitution of PRODEPE,
which will promote poverty alleviation on poor, urban and rural
subsidized primary schools;



- ix -
(c) facilitating the decentralization process, through the
participation of teachers and school principals in the design of
quality improvement projects, of supervisors, the municipal and
provincial authorities, and the provincial education Councils :In
project selection; and
'd) increasing the institutional managerial capabilities by
supporting: (i) the MINEDUC in translating educational policies and
strategies into concrete programmatic actions, targeting resources
to deprived schools, and periodically monitoring sector achievements
and problems through improved jector programming, budgeting,
information and analysis skills at its central, regional, and
provincial levels of decision making; (ii) the municipalities in
executing mnore effectively educational programs derived from the
education sector policies, and administering more efficiently the
resources of their assigned municipal schools; (iii) school
principals and proprietors in implementing technical-pedagogical
supervision and school management; and (iv) the timely detection
through the education information network of possible wastage in
resource allocation and resultant better deployment of teachers and
other educational resources.
Risks and Safeguards:   The major risks to project implementation are:
(a) possible distortions in targeting project interventions; and
(b) recentralization of education services. These risks will be
reduced by: (a) implementing precise targeting criteria to benefit
deprived urban and rural schools; (b) adopting suitable operational
and procedural manuals and training for provincial and municipal
education authorities, school principals, and supervisors; (c) care-
fully selecting and training key personnel participating in the
Project Implementation Unit, and in PRODEPE's disbursement and
monitoring; (d) promoting the participation of the municipalities
and communities in project implementation; and (e) close supervision
by the Bank.

DEFINITIONS
Crude Death Rate:                   Number of deaths per 1,000 population in a given
year.
Infant Mortality Rate:              Number of deaths of infants under one year of age
in a given year per 1,000 live births in that
year.
Life Expectancy at Birth:           Average number of years an infant would live if
the current age/sex-specific mortality trends
prevailing at the time of birth were to continue.
Rate of Population Growth:          Rate at which a population is increasing (or
decreasing) in a given year due to a surplus (or
deficit) of births over deaths, expressed as a
percentage of the total population.
Total Fertility Rate:               Average number of children whc would be born alive
to a woman during her lifetime if she were to pass
through her child-bearing years conforming to the
age-specific fertility rates of a given year.
Net Enrollment Ratio:               Percentage of children of a given age group
enrolled in schools at a particular level of
education.
Terminal Efficiency Rate:           Ratio between the number of students exiting 8th
grade in a given year T and the number of students
entering grade one in year T-8 (close approxima-
tion of the percentage of students of a given
cohort who completed primary school within the
eight year period).
Municipal:                          Educational establishments administered by the
municipalities of the state.
Private Subsidized:                 Private educaAional establishments that receive
subsidies from the state.
Private Paid:                       Private educational establishments that receive no
subsidies from the state.
Total Federal Education Spending:  The yearly disbursement of resources to the
MINEDUC, its regional and provincial offices, its
centralized and decentralized sectoral institu-
tions, as well as the subsidies for municipal and
private-subsidized preschool, primary, secondary,
and post secondary institutions. It also includes
spending on special education, adult education,
and culture.
Total Education Spending:           The Federal Education Spending plus the municipal
ow-.-resources allocated to education as well as
p':ivate, nonsubsidized spending at all levels of
education. It also includes investments in
education, allocated through the Ministry of
Interior.

I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The current Chilean Administration has adopted economic policies
consistent with the functioning of a private sector led market economy, while
promoting the improved delivery of targeted social services to enhance equity.
Productive activities in the economy are diversifying rapidly, as Chile
reduces its earlier dependence on natural resource extraction, increases the
international competitiveness of its value-added exports, and develops its
service sector, mainly through technological innovation. In order to facili-
tate and accelerate this process, the Government wishes to upgrade flows of
human capital, starting with an effort to improve the efficiency, quality, and
equity of the primary education system. Moreover, over the past two decades,
Chile, has implemented a significant reform of the social sectors. The reform
redefined the role of the Government, decentralized major social development
responsibilities to the municipalities, promoted privatization, targeted
public social expenditures to low-income groups, and channelled resources to
municipal and private-subsidized schools.
1.02      Chile's quantitative educational indicators compare favorably to
higher income economies: average years of schooling now attained by Chileans
is among the highest in Latin America, and adult illiteracy is among the
lowest in the region. Access problems in primary education are now largely
resolved. However, despite these accomplishments, the Achievement Assessment
System (SINCE) and educational statistics demonstrate that the sector has not
fully translated educational policy reforms into higher levels of quality,
efficiency, and equity, especially among low-income urban and rural groups.
Student achievement demonstrates low level mastery of objectives maintained
for the prescribed curriculum and is therefore not likely to contribute to the
quality of human resources necessary for a dynamically developing country.
Educational effectiveness has been hindered by the ambiguously defined joint
responsibilities brought by the reforms of the last decade between the
Ministry of Education (MINEDUC) and the municipalities. Furthermore, the
subsidy system implemented with the education reforms did not fully respond to
wide differentials between municipalities in the unit costs of delivering
educational services; neitner did it stimulate private investments in rural
and adult education. It thus exacerbated inequities in access to quality
education, especially between rural and urban children. At the same time,
adoption of high-cost preschool models has slowed considerably the expansion
of coverage of such programs.
1.03      Within the framework reviewed above, the Chilean Government has
already mobilized substantially more resources for social services through the
tax reform law and national budget redistributicn. It has: (a) established
the Provincial Education Councils (PEC) as advisory and participatory bodies;
(b) sent to Congress legislation to establish a professional Teachers Statute
(TS) to provide monetary incentives for inservice teacher training and for
teaching in deprived primary schools, and to amend the formula for educational
subsidies to ensure improved distribution of resources to small rural schools;
and (c) implemented specific actions targeted to low-income subsidized primary
schools to improve education quality and foster education innovations in
150 targeted primary subsidized schools. Finally, the Chilean educational
authorities have adopted, as a national policy, the enhancement of the effi-
ciency, quality and equity of primary education, especially for lower income
rural and urban students. To complement this policy MINEDUC has decided to:
(a) expand coverage of preschool education; (b) strengthen the institutional
capacity of the MINEDUC and other authorities involved in education; and
(c) assess alternative cost-effective approaches to secondary education nceds.



- 2-
II. THE EDUCATION SECTOR
A. Overview
Organization of the Educational Sector
2.01      The decentralized and well developed Chilean education system begins
with preschool for children under six years old. Preschool education is a
noncompulsory service offered by an array of public and private institutions
to promote children's social, psychomotor, and emotional development. Primary
education consists of an eight-year compulsory cycle for children 6 to 13
years old. Secondary education is furnished to students 14 to 17 years old
who have completed primary education. It involves a four-year cycle divided
into two tracks: (a) a scientific-humanistic curriculum that prepares
students for further studies through college; and (b) a technical-vocational
curriculum to prepare for work immediately after graduation. Special or
differentiated education is given to children 6 to 14 years old with learning
disabilities. Adult education programs are directed to adults 16 years and
older who want to learn to read and write or who need to complete primary or
secondary education.
2.02       MINEDUC carries its legally mandated functions of policy setting,
supervision, evaluation, and financial monitoring through its central,
regional, and provincial departments, largely independent of the municipa-
lities, which are in charge of the daily operation of the public schools.
Educational services are provided by the municipalities and private
proprietors, with the MINEDUC monitoring the quality of the services
delivered. The 334 municipalities provide educational services through their
Education Departments (280) or Corporaciones (54). Close to 15,500 schools
provide preschool, primary, secondary, and differentiated education in Chile.
The description and organization charts of the MINEDUC, regional, and the
provincial education departments appear in Annex 2.
Educational Reform of 1980
2.03      In 1980, Chile had a well-developed educational system that was one
of the oldest and most sophisticated in Latin America. MINEDUC provided
nearly all preschool, primary and secondary education and heavily subsidized
the two public and six private universities.  MINEDUC's 13 Regional Secre-
tariats (SEREMIS) were responsible for the administration and operation of all
public schools in the country.
2.04      At that time, the Government decided that such a highly centralized
system was not appropriate to respond to the problems of low efficiency and
low quality education. To address these concerns, the Government implemented
an administrative reform of the educational system. While the pre-reform
system awarded funds to schools and universities based on historical fiscal
allocations for teacher salaries and operating costs, the new system2
channelled resources to each municipality based on average monthly student
attendance. More specifically, this administrative reform consisted of:
(a) transferring the preschool, primary, and secondary schools to the
country's 325 municipalities (currently 334); (b) transferring a proportion of
the vocational secondary schools to private nonprofit organizations
("corporaciones") created by associations of employers; and (c) encouraging



-3-
through a subsidy payment per student ("subvenci6n") private individuals and
Non-Governmental Organizations (NGOs) to create tuition-free schools. Under
this new concept, the municipalities became completely autonomous in the
administration of material, financial, and human resources, as well as the
maintenance of schools.
2.05      The transfer of schools to the municipalities considerably reduced
the size of MINEDUC from 20,000 employees in 1980 to about 3,000 employees in
1990,L distributed by category as follows: managers, 14 percent; profes-
sional and technician, 49 percent; and clerical and services personnel,
37 percent. While the reform process brought a clear division of responsi-
bility between municipalities and the MINEDUC in some areas, ambiguously
stated responsibilities led to uncertainties in other areas. School personnel
administration, including hiring and firing of teachers and other school
personnel, is a municipal responsibility. Annual performance assessments of
teachers and other school personnel are carried out by municipalities, but
appear to be pro forma in many cases. Municipalities are currently in charge
of providing all support services, including diagnostic testing and libraries,
and making decisions about services provided to special categories of students
(e.g., those with learning disabilities). Joint responsibilities of municipa-
lities and central government agencies (including MINEDUC, the Ministries of
the Interior, Finance, and National Planning) include budgeting, accounting
controls, the management of resources to cover operational deficits (resulting
from frequent cases when municipal educational expenditures exceed income from
per student subsidies), and decisions about new construction and major
improvements in buildings and equipment. The Central Government also inter-
venes if municipal deficits are incurred, since municipalities are not
permitted to accumulate debt. Added to its oversight of municipal current
expenditures, the Government exercises tight control over municipal expendi-
tures for construction and school improvements via the regional offices of the
Ministry of Planning (MIDEPLAN).
Enrollment
2.06      Total pre-university enrollment for the school year 1989 amounted to
almost 3 million students attending over 15,000 schools (Annex 3, Table 1):
7.2 percent were registered in preschool, 66.8 percent in primary, 24.9 per-
cent in secondary (18.1 percent of which are in the scientific-humanistic
track and 6.8 percent in the technical-vocational track), and 1.1 percent in
differentiated education (Table 2, Annex 3). The Metropolitan Area (RM) of
Santiago encompassed 38.2 percent of the total enrollment; the regions of
Valparaiso (V), Concepcion (VIII), and Puerto Montt (X) comprised another
30.7 percent of the student population, with the remaining 31.1 percent
scattered throughout the other nine regions (Table 2, Annex 3). The municipal
schools serviced 58.7 percent of the total student population; the private
subsidized schools 32.1 percent; and the private nonsubsidized schools
9.2 percent (Table 3, Annex 3). The urban student population (in communities
with 301 or more inhabitants) represented 87.3 percent of the total. Female
1/   Out of the 3,000 employees, 21 percent are located at the MINEDUC in
Santiago; the rest are in the SEREMIS and the Provincial education
departments.



- 4 -
enrollment took nearly a 50 percent share of the total. Unlike many other
developing countries, gender discrimination in the Chilean educational system
is no longer an issue (Table 4, Annex 3). The regions of La Serena (IV),
Rancagua (VI), Talca (VII), Temuco (IX) and Puerto Mont (X), contain large
segments of rural and ethnic student populations (Table 4, Annex 3). A
breakdown of attendance by family income quintile shows a concentration of
low-income students in the municipal schools and of higher income students in
nonsubsidized private institutions (Table 5, Annex 3).
2.07      In the 1980s, total enrollment grew annually only 0.6 percent, i.e.,
from 2.84 million students in 1981 to 2.97 million in 1989. This limited
expansion was caused by a decrease of 7.1 percent in primary education
enrollment, even though there was an increase in the preschool, secondary, and
differentiated education enrollments (Table 6, Annex 3). The primary enroll-
ment decrease was mainly due to a decline in birthrate from 1.9 percent in
1970 to 1.67 percent in 1987, and a reduction in dropout rates (paras. 2.09
and 2.20). Net primary enrollment of the cohort of 6 to 13 years old has
remained very high for the past 20 years, increasing from 93 percent in 1970
to a peak of 98 percent in 1985, and slightly decreasing to its current level
of 95 percent.
2.08      Preschool Education.  Enrollment in preschool education has grown
rapidlv over the past decade totalling over 330,000 by 1989 (Tables 6 and 7,
Annex 3). Of these, 33.9 percent were registered in the municipal schools,
23.5 percenit in the private subsidized schools, 15.3 percent in National
Kindergarden Board (JUNJI; JUNTA Nacional de Jardines Infantiles), schools,
12.4 percent in INTEGRA schools, 1.8 percent in Jardines Familiares,
3.1 percent in NGO schools, and 9.9 percent in the paid-private schools.
These enrollments represent 28.4 percent of the cohort of 2- to 5-year-olds;
69.3 percent of total enrollment corresponded to children age 5; 20.7 percent
to 4 year olds, and the remaining 24.5 percent were 2 to 3 year olds (Table 8,
Annex 3). The number of teachers and paraprofessionals employed in preschool
education in 1989 is shown in Table 9, Annex 3.
2.09      Primary Education  In 1989, enrollment totaled 1,987,758 students,
representing 94.6 percert of the age group 6-13 years (Table 10, Annex 3).
Only a very sm.l1 number of children, most of them in need of special or
differentiated education, do not enroll in the primary schools. Enrollment
has decreased 7.1 percent since 1981, due to the decline in birthrates and a
reduction in dropout rates from 8 percent in 1981 to 2.7 percent in 1989.
After the 1980 reform, enrollment in the private-subsidized schools doubled as
their primary education student population rose from 14 percent of total
enrollment to 31 percent during this period. The increase came at the expense
of enrollment in the municipal schools, whose share decreased from 80.0
percent in 1980 to 62 percent in 1989. The enrollment share in paid-private
schools increased only slightly from 6 to 7 percent (Table 11, Annex 3).
2.10      Secondary Education.  Total enrollment in secondary education in
1989 reached 742,010 students (about 81.6 percent of the 14 to 17 year olds
cohort), or 37 percent more than in 1980. Enrollment in private-subsidized
schools increased almost three times in the last decade (Table 12, Annex 3).
Over 70 percent of secondary students chose the scientific-humanistic track
during the last decade. Two factors have contributed to this: (a) the



educational laws enacted in 1980 and 1981 that opened new alternatives for
admission to higher education; and (b) a high school student's expectations of
getting a better job by earning a university degree.
2.11      Higher Education.  During 1989 there were almost 230,000 students
enrolled in higher education institutions, an increase of 102.0 percent with
respect to the 1981 enrollment. The 34 universities absorbed 57 percent of
the enrollment; the 133 technological centers, 30 percent; and the 53
professional institutes, 13 percent. Twenty-two higher education institu-
tions out of the universe of 220 institutions received Government subsidies
during 1989. About 58.0 percent of the students were enrolled in the
subsidized, higher education institutions.
Adult Illiteracy and Years of Schooling
2.12      Chile reduced the illiteracy rate of its adult population from
16.4 percent in 1960 to 5.6 in 1989. According to the 1970 and 1982
population censuses, the average number of years of schooling of the Chilean
population increased from 4.3 to 7.7 in that period, with current estimates at
8.5 years of schooling. According to the most recent National Socioeconomic
Survey (Encuesta de Caracterizaci6n Socioecon6mica Nacional--CASEN) data, the
national schooling average for children under 15 years old is about 10 years,
and for household-heads in the fifth quintile is almost 12 years. This gives
Chile the second lowest illiteracy rate in Latin America and one of the
highest years of schooling rate. However, considerable variations exist in
average level of educational attainment by Region and by income quintile, with
the lowest rates not surprisingly in the poorest areas and social groups of
the country.
Women in Development
2.13      Chile has an outstanding record concerning the issue of Women in
Development (WID). No gender bias exists in school enrollment in all the
educational levels (para. 2.06 and Annex 3, Table 4). In its long tradition
of textbooks distribution for all primary school students, the Chilean
Government, through MINEDUC, has explicitly stated no gender biases as a
precondition to procure the publication, production, and distribution of new
editions from the private sector.
Educational Expenditures and Finance
2.14      Public spending for education in Chile has declined during the last
decade. Education budget's share of GNP fell to 2.5 percent in 1990,
declining from almost 13 percent of total public spending in 1981 to 8.1 in
1990 (Table 1 and Chart 1, Annex 4), The decentralization of education funds
to private-subsidized schools ar.d municipalities and the financing of private
and municipal subsidized schools through subvention payments, now approximate
60.0 percent of the education budget (Table 2, Annex 4). As noted in Table 3
and Chart 2 in Annex 4, there has been a dramatic decline in higher
education's share of total public spending in the sector.



2.15      Even though government has given priority to primary education,
public spending per student has declined in real terms during the 1980's
(Table 4, Annex 4). Capital spending has represented a small share of sector
spending (Table 5, Annex 4). Due to a number of factors, including loss of
students to newly-formed private schools, municipalities experienced deficits
in their education departments when the amounts provided through government
subsidies failed to cover educational costs. Municipalities have responded by
transferring funds from their own resources to bridge the gap. The total
contribution of municipalities to educational funding amounted to 3.2 percent
of total education spending in 1990 (Table 6, Annex 4) and is less than
7.5 percent of the amount transferred to the municipalities through subsidies.
To address the issues of adequate and equitable funding, a reform of the
Subvention Law (SL) is now before the legislature. Its intention, among other
objectives, is to bring subsidy payments more closely in line with actual
costs and to adjust more adequately for differences between communities in
terms of population dispersion and the cost of providing educational services.
The intended result is a "regularization" of educational finance in subsidized
public and private schools, whereby Central Government payments for education
would flow through the education budget rather than through special alloca-
tions to municipalities to cover deficits. This adjustment will certainly
decrease MINEDUC's cost to administer the subsidy scheme, and personnel time
will be freed to focus on improved quality issues.
2.16       Personnel costs dominate sector spending.  A Teachers' Statute (TS)
already enacted (Law 19070, dated July 1, 1991) will improve teachers' pay and
working conditions, provide monetary incentives for inservice training and
teaching in deprived and isolated schools, and make other changes in the legal
structure affecting the profession. The TS is part of the government's
commitment to improve conditions in the sector and contributes to better
educational quality (see Annex 5, paras. 3 and 4, for a brief discussion).
The TS will call for increases in recurrent expenditure beginning in 1991, for
which budget allocation has already been provided, rising to an estimated
total of 38.7 billion pesos in 1995 (Table 2, Annex 5). This increase, while
substantial, is sustainable. Annex 5, Table 1 demonstrates that projected
growth of educational recurrent spending, including the impact of the project
and the TS, will represent 11.6 percent of public spending in 1995 declining
slightly thereafter.
2.17      The decline in public spending for education has been counter-
balanced by increaaed private sector payments. The share of private
contribution to educational costs has increased from roughly 21 percent of
total sector finance (including central education, municipalities, and private
education) to 31.6 percent between 1985 and 1990. Most of the private share
of sector spending and most of the increase in private payments is at the
university level (Table 7 and Chart 3, Annex 4). Estimated private higher
education payments grew from 14.7 percent of total educational spending to
over 24 percent (or roughly three-fourths of total private funding) between
1985 and 1990. Combined municipal and private payments have grown during the
period of educational decentralization, and accounted for almost one-third of
total sector finance in 1990. These payments did not, however, fully
compensate for the decline in public finance of education. Total sector
finance, including public and private, declined from 8.64 percent of GNP to
3.7 percent from 1985 to 1990 (Table 6, Annex 4).



- 7 -
B. Sectoral Issues
2.18      The main education sector issues are:  (a) unacceptably low primary
education efficiency; (b) unsatisfactory education quality in both academic
achievement (output) and learning environment (educational inputs);
(c) inequities in the delivery and quality of services; (d) weak managerial,
financial, and administrative capacity; and (e) shortcomings of post-primary
education.
Unacceptably Low Primary Education Efficiency
2.19      Although Chile has by and large already solved the traditional
problems of literacy and access to primary education, unsatisfactory efficien-
cy at this educational level continues to be an issue. Late primary school
entrance, high repetition and dropout in primary schools, and the existence of
quite a large number of "incomplete" primary rural schools not offering the
full eight primary grades, have the greatest impact on low promotion and
completion rates.
2.20      Late Primary School Entrance.  Figures for 1989 suggest that some
44.0 percent of the 6 year olds enroll in the first grade each year; another
50.0 percent join at age seven, and the rest (about 6 percent), at age 8 or
above. Available data show that late entrance, however small, increases
repetition rates, and decreases the completion rates of primary education to
the point where about 17.7 percent of students enrolled lag behind the
standard age for each grade in primary education by one or more yearss which
increases to 23.4 percent at the secondary level (Table 1, Annex 6).
2.21      Repetition and Dropout Rates in Primary Education.  The average
repetition rate for primary education at the national level in 1988, as
calculated by UNESC0-OREALC,L was 12.2 percent (Table 2, Annex 6). It was
higher between the fourth and eighth grades (average 14 percent) and in the
regions with heavy concentrations of rural and ethnic students, such as Talca
and Temuco (Table 3, Annex 6). Private paid schools have shown the lowest
repetition rate, and thus the highest promotion rates. Municipal schools
repetition rate is slightly better than private subsidized schools (Table 2,
Annex 6). Dropout rates vary from 2 percent in first grade to 8.0 percent by
the eighth grade. The dropout rate is higher in municipal and private
subsidized schools than in paid private ones. The dropout rate in rural and
ethnic regions (such as Region X) is more than double the national average
(Tables 2 and 3, Annex 6).
2.22      Incomplete Primary Rural Schools.  About 7 percent of the total
primary schools in Chile (568) offer five grades or less, and 48 percent
(3,814) do not offer the last two grades of the full primary cycle (Table 4,
Annex 6). The existence of these so-called incomplete primary schools, mostly
located in rural and ethnic communities, keep many students from completing
their primary cycle.
2/   Calculated by E. Schiefelbein from UNESCO-OREALC with data made available
by MINEDUC Planning Division, and correcting for reenrollment which tends
to understate repetition.



2.23      Promotion and Completion Rates in Primary Education.  According to
UNESCO/OREALC estimates, the average number of students/years required in
Chile for promotion to the next primary grade is 1.2 to 1.3 years; one-fifth
of the total enrollment will complete the primary level after repeating two or
more times, resulting in a total of almost 12.4 years expended for the 8-year
cycle in municipal schools (8.8 years in the paid-private primary schools)
(Table 5, Annex 6). Here again it appears that significant scope for improve-
ment exists. While Chile's national completion rates are among the highest in
the Latin America and the Caribbean Region, the cumulative effects of late
entrance, repetition, and dropout rates, and the incomplete primary schools,
mean that only 57.5 percent of the initial student cohort finish the eighth
basic education cycle. Some of the specific causes of poor performance are:
lack of personalized instruction, no periodic student evaluation during the
school year, inadequate time spent by teachers on learning activities, and
poor supervision, especially by school principals. There is also evidence of
an increasing gap in quality caused by the differential access to educational
materials, textbooks, and computers in primary schools. In addition,
differencas occur by socioeconomic group in terms of child care and stimula-
tion after school hours. There are indications of age heterogeneity in the
classroom, originated mostly by late entrance to first grade and continuous
repetition, which makes it more difficult for the teacher to organize meaning-
ful learning experiences.  Finally, the limited bilingual primary education
programs, are insufficient to serve ethnic groups, which represent perhaps
5.0 percent of the primary student population.
2.24      Repetition Rates in Secondary Education.  About 85 percent of
primary education graduates continue to the secondary level. Repetition rates
for the secondary education vary from 5.4 percent for paid-private schools to
15.3 percent for the municipal schools, whereas dropout rates climb respec-
tively from 3 to almost 11 percent. There has been a decline in secondary
repetition and dropout rates since 1980. The secondary completion rate is
approximately 71 percent,2L which is equivalent to an internal efficiency of
41 percent of the initial primary school cohort./ It takes an average of
5.6 years for a municipal secondary student to graduate, and 4.4 years for a
paid-private secondary student (Table 5, Annex 6).
Unsatisfactory Educational Quality
2.25      The unsatisfactory education quality in both academic achievement
(education output)--shown by a decrease in Spanish (Table 6, Annex 6) and
mathematics (Table 7, Annex 6) in the 1982 to 1988 period--and in the learning
environment (education inputs) has been mainly caused by: (a) insufficient
supervision and monitoring by school principals and supervisors; (b) deficien-
cies in the primary education texts and in the curriculum on which they are
3/   Ratio between the number of students exiting secondary level (grade 12)
in a given year T and the number of students entering secondary education
(grade 9) in year T-4.
4/   Ratio between the number of students exiting secondary level (grade 12)
in a given year T and the number of students entering grade 1 in primary
schools in year T-12.



based; (c) lack of other supplementary educational materials; (d) lack of
skillful, well-trained, and highly motivated teaching force; and (e) insuffi-
cient use of existing academic achievement test reAults to guide the decision-
making process at all institutional levels, and to target compensatory
pedagogical measures to high-risk students.
2.26      Educational Supervision and Monitoring.  Educational supervision and
monitoring is a key instrument to imprcie the quality of the services provided
if performed primarily by the school principal in conjunction with and
supported by the pedagogical supervisor. Educational supervision in the
Chilean preschool and primary education system is deficient basically for two
reasons: school principals are not trained adequately to supervise pedagogi-
cally their teaching force nor do they have the skills and resources needed to
implement successfully such a function in their schools. On the other hand,
the super-visory labor force is limited. There are only 40 preschool and 375
primary education supervisors to monitor almost 4,200 preschools and over
8,500 primary education schools in the country, comprising a combined teaching
force of 77,000. Supervisors usually lack transportation, facilities, and
resources. They are also overloaded with other administrative tasks not
directly related to technical-pedagogical activities. As a consequence, most
schools, especially the rural ones, do not receive the adequate pedagogical
supervision they require during the school year.
2.27      Textbooks and Curriculum.  There are several deficiencies in the
primary education texts and in the curriculum on which they are based. These
deficiencies can be particularly significant when teachers themselves lack
mastery of the material, and when students lack supplemental learning opportu-
nities in the home or at school. Some of the written learning objectives are
not presented in the detail needed to test whether each objective has been
accomplished by the students. Another deficiency is that texts do not
necessarily go beyond straightforward application of the materials presented.
Current textbooks also lack complementary teaching material relevant to the
rural areas. In this environment, an improved curriculum and textbooks may be
the most cost-effective means to improve learning of the type needed to
prepare students for the increasingly modern and technologically changing
economy. The primary education curricula, textbooks, and teaching materials
do not contain gender biases. Furthermore, the MINEDUC has explicit'ly stated
no gender biases as a precondition to procure the publication, production, and
distribution of new textbooks and teaching materials. No cost-recovery
policies on textbooks and/or other teaching materials has so far been
implemented by the Chilean authorities, yet the current administration has
agreed to implement a targeted cost-recovery policy (paras. 3.17 and 3.39).
2.28      Supplementarv Educational Materials.  Municipal primary education
schools tend to lack supplementary educational materials such as globes, maps,
charts, educational toys, supplemental texts, and workbooks. Likewise, more
costly but effective resources such as libraries, classroom reading resources,
science kits, and computers are rarely in evidence. The above situation also
prevails at the preschool and secondary schools. Private subsidized and
nonsubsidized schools have a somewhat larger, but still inadequate quantity of
didactic materials.



- 10 -
2.29      Teaching Labor Force.  An important factor to improve quality of
education is to incorporate into the educational system a skillful, well-
trained, and highly-motivated teaching force. According to the Chilean
education authorities, there is ample room to improve the above situation with
the current 9,000 preschool and 67,000 primary school teachers. Prevailing
teacher salaries range from a low of 8 times the present individual student
subsidy (most frequently observed in recently established private-subsidized
schools not affiliated with churches or nonprofit organizations) to a high of
some 20 times the student subsidy (most frequently observed in municipal and
NGO schools). Average primary school teacher salaries decreased 48 percent in
real terms in the municipal schools and 61 percent in the private-
subsidized schools in the 1981 to 1990 period.5 Given the low level of
teacher salaries, the quality of those now applying for teaching positions is
probably also decreasing. As a result, effective teacher time on tasks has
diminished, as indicated by the Chilean education authorities.
2.30      Beginning in 1987, teachers w7ere required to have college degrees;
however, many teachers without degrees were permitted to obtain them through
relatively short correspondence courses. There is evidence of insufficientI
preservice and inservice training and upgrading programs and incentives for
preschool and primary education teachers and paraprofessionals. This
situation is more apparent in the case of the paraprofessionals employed in
some of the preschool modalities.
2.31      Academic Achievement Assessment.  Between 1982 and 1984, the MINEDUC
administered two sets of tests nationwide--known as Programa de Evaluacion del
Rendimiento Escolar (PER)--to 390,000 fourth and eighth graders in 3,200
schools. These tests were designed to measure cognitive achievement in the
areas of reading, mathematics, social sciences, and natural sciences. A new
achievement test, an updated and corrected version of PER known as SIMCE, was
applied in 1988 to 233,000 fourth graders in 5,600 primary schools; in 1989
the test was again applied, this time to 193,000 eighth graders in 4,600
schools. About 3,700 primary rural schools with enrollments of six students
or less in fourth or eighth grades were not included in the 1988 and 1989
applications of SIMCE, because the current assessment instrument is not
appropriate for small groups. SIMCE has been transferred from the Catholic

of 1984. CPEIP's training program for 1991 aims at 16,000 principals and
teachers.
