University (UC) to MINEDUC, including key intellectual leadership familiar
with its design, operation, and processing.
2.32      Test results by school type show that the paid-private schools
scored highest, followed by the private-subsidized, and then the municipal
schools (Table 8, Annex 6). These results, although suggestive, should be
interpreted with caution, since the information was not adequately disaggre-
gated for further analysis. PER (1982) and SIMCE (1988) results for Spanish
and mathematics show no improvement in the quality of education during the
1980s (Tables 6 and 7, Annox 6). Furthermore, the gap between the highest
scores in the Spanish test, shown in the paid-private school, and the lowest
scores in the high-risk municipal schools, widened in the 1982 to 1988 period.
Current academic achievement in the Chilean primary education systems is below
the standards called for in the sector policy (paras. 2.50-2.52, and Annex 7).
2.33      In spite of SIMCE's sophisticated tests, procedures, and organiza-
tion, they are not currently used as an educational evaluation system. This
is because SIMCE does not provide the information required to the key inter-
ested groups. MINEDUC and the municipal education authorities need the data
for correcting educational policies and strategies targeted to ameliorate
inequities in the quality of the educational services provided, especially
among lower-income groups. School teachers, principals, and supervisors could
utilize the information for upgrading teaching and learning practices, and
developing more suitable teaching and learning materials. Finally, parents
would find the data useful for information on progress of their children, and
possible school choice. The same is true for other evaluation studies
undertaken, such as those implemented by the UC under the sponsorship of
UNICEF to evaluate JUNJI's preschool models of jardines infantiles and
jardines infantilas fariliares.Y
Inequities in the Delivery and Quality of Services
2.34      Inequities in the delivery and quality of services are manifested by
regional disparities in: (a) preschool participation; (b) children nutrition-
al status; (c) primary school promotion rates; (d) achievement raten-
(e) deployment of specialized and compensatory education resources; and
(f) average years of schooling and literacy rates of the adult population.
2.35      Ineguities in Preschool Participation.  A recent study undertaken by
UNICEF in Chile found that close to 73 percent of children under 6 years old
living in poor urban and rural areas are not served by any modality of
preschool education (Table 9, Annex 6). Of those not served, 23 percent live
in conditions of extreme poverty. Children with psychomotor and social skills
deficits are at higher risk of repetition and dropout during primary school.
7/   The evaluation shows the positive contribution of these preschool
alternatives to improve the child's nutritional status, as well as its
social and psychomotor development. Yet, these studies have had little
effect on educational policy, the exception being the formulation of the
preschool component of this project. Evaluaci6n v provecci6n del
Rrograma CADEL. Instituto de Economia, Pontificia Universidad Cat6lica
de Chile, Santiago; 1989.



- 12 -
2.36      Disparities in Children's Nutritional Status.  Although Chile has
made rem,kable achievements in reducing malnutrition among young children,
there still are distinct subgroups potentially at risk from suffering from
moderate to severe malnutrition, according to weight-for-age standards.
Infants and preschoolers, particularly from low-income families, and school
children not participating in any preventive health control, feeding program,
and/or dayeare programs mainly conform the groups at risk. National preva-
lence rates mask often sign!'4cant disparities in child nutritional statua by
geographic area of residence, age group, and family socioeconomic characteris-
tics. The prevalence of malnutrition among children in select, predominantly
rural, southern regions of Chile is more than double that of generally large
urbanized northern regions.
2.37      Rezional Disparities in Primary School Promotion Rates.  Unsatis-
factory national primary education promotion rates, averaging 42.5 percent for
the initial cohort of students, hit hardest among deprived children from low-
income households, thus indicating an area for priority attention. About one-
third of first-grade students living in poor rural and urban areas may be
repeating that grade. Although at the national level some 10.7 percent of all
first-grade students repeat it, and some 10 to 16 percent repeat at least once
between the second and eighth grades, repetition of lower-income students
(half the population) is nearer 20 to 25 percent.
2.38      Regional Disparities in Primary School Achievement.  The 1988
nationwide SIMCE test of fourth graders also revealed some regional inequities
(Table 10, Annex 6). For example, school performance in the urban poor and
rural regions of Temuco (IX) and Puerto Montt (X) was half that of more
affluent zones. Furthermore, the different applications of primary achieve-
ment tests (PER and SIMCE) in 1982, 1984, 1988, and 1989, show that the
average cognitive performance in Spanish and mathematics of primary students,
especially poor students, is not improving (Tables 6 and 7, Annex 6). Statis-
tical studies based on the information provided by the evaluation tests found
that socioeconomic factors are the main determinants of school achievement in
Chile, ad is the case in many other countries. The implication is a need for
stronger compensatory measures, such as early childhood stimulation, motiva-
tion, and personality building, which are capable of modifying variables at
school level and eventually improving student achievement, especially among
poor students. School pedagogical supervision, primarily undertaken by school
principals with the -echnical support of qualified and experienced school
supervisors, should also be redesigned to target schools most in need. Urban
poor and rural schools should be provided with more textbooks, teaching aids
and reading materials.
2.39      Inequities in the De2lovment of Specialized and ComDensatorv
Education Resources. Municipalities, especially those with relatively low
enrollment levels, often lack the specialized resources and expertise needed
for the design, operation, and supervision of special education programs
(geared fundamentally to those students with various types of learning
disabilities) that can very quickly become repeaters and eventual dropouts
from the primary system. The per student subsidies provided by the Government
for this type of program are clearly inadequate to cover costs. Municipali-
ties can provide these services only by cross-subsidizing them with funds
intended for regular instruction or by providing supplementary funds.



- 13 -
2.40      Regional Disparities in Averaze Years of Schooling and Literacy
Rates of the Adult Population. There are marked signs of existing social
inequities in the Chilean society as measured by the average years of school-
ing and literacy rates of the adult population. According to the most recent
CASEN survey, there is a 3.8 years difference in average years of schooling
between Chileans in the lowest income quintile and those in the highest.
Relatively larger differences exist between Chileans 15 years and older in
first and fifth quintile urban families (5.9 years) than among those from
first and fifth quintile rural families (3.1 years). The above are marked
signs of existing social inequities in the Chilean society.
Weak ManaRerial. Financial, and Administrative Capacity
2.41      The educational sector has insufficient institutional ce?ability to
strengthen and consolidate ongoing education reforms to assure educational
efficiency, quality, and equity improvements, especially among low-income
urban and rural groups, and thus fortify the decentralization process. This
situation stems primarily from the limited ability of: (a) MINEDUC to
translate effectively educational policies and strategies into concrete
programmatic actions, target resources to deprived schools through the
municipalities, and periodically monitor sector achievements; (b) the munici-
palities to execute educational programs derived from sector policies, and
administer efficiently the resources of their assigned municipal schools; and
(c) schEol administrators to implement adequately technical-pedagogical
superv.aion and school management. Moreover, the lack of a timely, trust-
worthy, and relevant Educational Information System (EIS) has not allowed the
Chilean educational authorities to ascertLin the impact of a decreasing
primary enrollment on resource utilization, resulting in possible overstaffing
in some subsidized schools. The lack of a ?rofessional teachers statute
regulating the teachers' out-of-class pedtgogical activities, the inservice
teacher training inducement and promotion, as well as appropriate reviews in
the current subvention law (SL) to avoid inequities in resource allocation to
small, mostly rural primary schools, has firther weakened the educational
institutional capacity.
2.42      Translation of Educational Policies and StrateRies Into Concrete
Programmatic Actions. In order to properly u ilize funds being allocated to
the sector it is critical to improve the avai.bility of timely and reliable
management information that could guide policy-making and targeting of
resources. Due to lack of appropriate hardware, software, and technical
personnel, the central, regional, provincial, municipal, and local (school)
education authorities have not been able to make timely and relevant decisions
based on the current educational statistical data base and sector financing
information system. In addition, the weak and understaffed system of school
supervision and inspection in MINEDUC's 40 Provincial Departments, and the
lack of planning and managerial skills in the municipalities has cast primary
and secondary schools almost adrift in a condition of quasi-autonomy. As a
consequence, the NINEDUC has been unable to ensure properly that curriculum
standards and guidelines are suitably applied and/or that investment resources
(targeted for school construction and maintenance) and other educational
inputs are correctly deployed by the municipalities i-. the schools. The
ineffective monitoring and supervisory system currently in place hinders the
timely implementation of corrective measures that would assure the provision
of better educational service3, especially for the poor.



- 14 -
2.43      Insufficlent Municipal Management Capability.  Municipal education
authorities, both at the DEM's and the Corporations, lack adequate procedures
and operational manuals on: (a) microplanning techniques for efficient
deployment and targeting of available resources; (b) personnel management;
(c) formulation of school construction, o luipping, and refurbishing projects;
and (d) financing, accounting, and auditing. Furthermore, some municipal
governments undersupply several important services that could enhance educa-
tional quality and improve the completion rates of primary education. There
seems to be limited information regarding proven and cost-effective education-
al techniques used in other countries. Tests for the assessment and diagnosis
of students with learning disabilities are very limited. There is a similar
lack of support for the education of indigenous groups for whom Spanish is a
second language. Primary facilities and equipment are generally adequate in
the better-off municipalities, but not in the deprived, mostly rural schools.
Certain facilities that could improve the auality of education are generally
lacking. These include science kits, libraries and classroom reading
resources, space to accommodate students with learning disabilities, and
computer-aided education laboratories. The quality of physical facilities in
the private-paid schools, although variable, is generally higher than in the
municipal and private-subsidized school system, where municipalities and
private proprietors have not been able, in general, to provide a systematic
and periodic maintenance. Finally, municipal and private-subsidized school
principals do not receive adequate training and advice on school management
and technical-pedagogical supervision.
2.44      Resources intended for school construction, equipment, and mainte-
nance are allocated by the Central Government to the Ministry of Education and
to the municipalities through the Ministry of Interior, plus a small addition-
al amount through the President's Social Fund and other sources. The total
amount of capital spending has been very low during the 1980's, ranging
between one to 2.0 percent of the total sector spending (Table 5, Annex 4).
Much of this came from an InterAmerican Development Bank (IDB) loan to support
the Ministry of Interior Development Fund. However, the fungibility of these
funds, the lack of sufficient funds to cover operating expenses, especially in
poor municipalities, and the total autonomy of the municipalities, leads in
some cases to such resources being used to cover school operating deficits or
investments in other activities.
2.45      Professional Teachers Statute.  The lack of a professional teacher
statute has restrained: (a) increasing teacher time on task and productivity;
(b) good teachers from working in disadvantaged schools; (c) teachers from
engaging in teaching skill-upgrading activities; (d) offsetting teachers real
wages decline due to cost of living increases (para. 2.28); and (e) offering
cost-effective opportunities for senior teachers to retire.U The Government
is effectively addressing these issues with a new TS which has high potential
benefits (para. 3.27).
8/   Teachers with 26 or more years of service represent 12.5 percent of the
teaching labor force.



- 15 -
2.46      Education Subsidies.  The present design of school subsidies, based
largely on the type of education provided and monthly average student atten-
dance, discriminates against small, typically rural schools with relatively
low student/teacher ratios. The inherently high fixed costs of education,
reflected in its teacher-intensive nature, make it difficult for small rural
schools to reach break-even points. For these cases, the current subsidy does
not provide for teaching materials, administration, and maintenance, and in
some cases, sufficient teacher salaries. Also, the subsidy does not stimulate
private investment in rural and adult education. Nor does it take into
account cost differentials by region, educational modality, or specialty
within a given educational level. The subsidy unjustifiably penalizes schools
for student absenteeism not directly imputable to the student, such as the
ones prompted by rain and flooding in the rural areas. The Executive Branch
initiative to modify the existing SL addresses the deficit issue of mostly
poor rural municipalities (para. 3.29).
ShortcominRs of Post-Primary Education
2.47      Shortcomings in post-primary education mainly refer to:  (a) the
secondary curriculum modes; and (b) equity in the delivery of secondary
education to low-income primary graduates.
2.48      Secondary Dual Curriculum Modes.  Secondary education enrollment has
expanded from 30 percent of the relevant age group in the early 1970s to about
82 percent at present. This enrollment increase has brought some problems
related to the difficulties of serving students with different levels of
preparation, a greater age range than in the past, and diverse career and
educational aspirations. The two track secondary education system has not:
(a) adapted itself to the changing needs and requirements; (b) planned and
implemented adequately its curriculum, resulting in an overloaded curriculum;
and (c) been evaluated. The gap between subsidized and non-subsidized schools
has consistently widened. The economic crisis has led to deterioration of
physical facilities, especially for scientific and technical subjects and up-
to-date educational materials. The various parties concerned with educational
policy and strategy have not been able to engage in fruitful dialogue on the
issues. There is a pervasive sense that secondary school education has
stagnated and fallen far behind Chile's needs in a modern and rapidly-changing
world, and that the time is propitious for a careful consideration of the
objectives and alternative strategies for this subsector.
2.49      Eguity in the Delivery of Secondary Education to Low-Income Primary
Graduates. Many of the problems of the subsector have to do with inequitable
delivery of educational services to these very groups. The recent CASEN data
showed that coverage and access continue to be lower for the low-income
stratum of society. Moreover, repetition and dropout rates are higher than
average in the schools that serve these groups. Critics argue that the study
plans designed for a smaller and more homogenous and elite student body are
inappropriate for a substantial portion of the present enrollees. Indicators
of achievement for secondary school students are largely confined to "academic
aptitude tests" that many universities require of applicants. It is these
tests that underscore the widening quality gap between public and private paid
secondary schools. There are indications that public secondary schools
serving students from lower-income families have more serious qualitative
problems than the average of public schools, although more information is
needed on this and other aspects of the secondary system.



- 16 -
C. Government Sectoral Obiectives and Stratep.y
2.50      The Chilean Government has assigned top priority to upgrading flows
of new human capital within an overall strategy of increasing Chile's competi-
tiveness in the international economy. Enhancing the efficiency, quality, and
equity of primary education, especially among lower-income students, is the
logical starting point for addressing this priority.
2.'51     The following strategies, basically targeted to deprived urban and
rural primary schools, have been approved by the Chilean authorities to
achieve stated educational policies: (a) expanding coverage and enhancing the
quality of preschool education to increase primary school preparedness and
reduce late entry, repetition, and dropout rates, especially for 5 year olds
living in poverty conditions; (b) establishing a pedagogical decentralization
program to induce the participation of teachers and school principals in
implementing school quality improvement plans in municipal and private-sub-
sidized primary schools; (c) strengthening the educational testing system to
evaluate and monitor student performance on a continuous basis; (d) enhancing
the quality of diverse educational inputs; (e) offering the full primary
eighth grade cycle in incomplete primary schools; (f) strengthening the
institutional capacity of the MINEDUC, regional and provincial departments,
the municipalities, to support the ongoing decentralization process; and
(g) assess alternative cost-effective approaches to meet the secondary
education needs of primary graduate students, especially from low-income urban
and rural areas. The policy and strategy matrix appears in Annex 7.
2.52      Implementation of the above strategy has already started.  The
MINEDUC implemented a "900 Deprived Primary Schools Program," under a grant
provided by the Nordic countries. This program aims to improve student
achievement among 160,000 first to fourth graders of low-income municipal and
private-subsidized schools that scored low on the SIMCE and have high repeti-
tion and dropout rates. This program comprises: (a) allocating teaching
materials; (b) refurbishing primary school buildings; (c) providing inservice
teacher and supervisor training; and (d) sustaining remedial workshops for
potential dropout and repeaters. The technical, managerial, and logistical
experience gained in this program since 1990, as well as the teaching and
learning materials developed will be transferred to the proposed project. In
addition, the Teacher Training and Educational Research Center (Centro de
Perfeccionamento, Experimentacion, e Investigaciones, y Tecnol6gica--CPEIP)
has launched a national program to induce the participation of supervisors,
teachers and principals of about 150 municipal and private subsidized schools
to develop quality improvement projects. The experience learned during its
implementation will be transferred to the proposed project, especially
concerning the pedagogical decentralization process (paras. 3.04 to 3.08).
Finally, to induce community involvement in the Chilean educational process,
PECs were established by Presidential Decree in each of the 40 Provinces of
Chile during the second quarter of 1990. These Councils have an education
advisory capacity and are participatory bodies comprising MINEDUC and
municipal authorities, teachers, and parents. Bylaws governing these Councils
have been enacted.



- 17 -
2.53      The financial capacity to carry out these programs and policies is
favorable. The Government has implemented a major tax reform that has
produced additional resources for the public sector as a whole. Education and
health have received a substantial proportion of the social sector allocation.
The tax reform went into operation in the last quarter of 1990 producing
additional revenues that were distributed as budget supplements during that
year. The education budget for 1991 of almost 255.5 billion pesos (Table 2,
Annex 4) shows an increase of 54.6 billion pesos (US$158.3 million) over the
budget for 1990.1  After adjustment for inflation, the increase in the
education budget between 1990 and 1991 in real terms was 11.4 percent. IWhile
this substantial increase in the education budget is a one-time step upward in
sector resources, and increases in the future will be in line with overall
increases in public revenues. The tax reform assures that adequate resources
will be available for counterpart funding to sustain the projects recurrent
costs and the TS after the project is completed. Recurrent expenditures for
education will increase from 8.9 to a maximum of 11.6 percent of estimated
total public spending during the period 1991-97 (Table 1, Annex 5). This in-
crease, to which the government is committed, does not result in unrealistic
requirements for financial resources.
2.54      The Government has issued a policy statement for the education
sector specifying: (a) the medium-term goals regarding equity, efficiency,
and quality of primary education; (b) the overall sector financing plan for
primary, secondary and tertiary levels of the education system; (c) the
responsibilities at various levels of Government and the private sector; and
(d) that the Government will allocate requisite financial resources to sustain
future education expenditures. The policy statement also includes: (a) a
quantification in line with the projections of the SAR, of the rate of the
annual increase in education expenditures required to achieve and sustain its
quality improvement program in a decentralized system; and (b) a statement of
intent to increase education expenditures accordingly, as long as there is no
marked deterioration in the macroeconomic or public finance situation. The
policy statement, which is available in the project files, will provide the
basis for the annual budget review (para. 4.09).
D. Bank Role and Strategv
2.55 Bank involvement in education in Chile began in 1965 with projects
supporting vocational training and primary education. The first two education
projects (Ln. 431-CH for US$2.7 million, and Ln. 666-CH for US$1.5 million in
1965 and 1970, respectively) were aimed at expanding the training capacity of
the National Institute for Vocational Training. The projects were success-
fully implemented and assisted in making this Institute an outstanding
training institution. The third project (Ln. 668-CH for US$7.0 million in
1970) supported the MINEDUC to expand and improve primary teacher training
colleges and agriculture secondary schools. This project was prematurely
implemented given that the country's policy basis was in its very formative
stages at that time. Furthermore, project implementation coincided with a
9/   Comparison between approved budgets in current pesos.



- 18 -
period of political turmoil in Chile with the result that many project
objectives were never achieved. Besides these three education projects, the
Bank has assisted the Chilean Government during the last two decades in the
following sectors: power (four projects), transportation (seven projects),
water and sewage (three projects), agriculture (four projects), industry and
mining (four projects), housing (two projects), and sectoral adjustment and
technical assistance (five projects). All of these projects assisted the
Chilean Government to attain macroeconomic stability, adopt a market economy,
liberalize trade, and promote private sector participation as the main engine
of economic growth. Some of these involved important components directed at
the social sector. Structural Adjustment Loan III aimed, for example, among
other objectives, at: (a) increasing the efficiency of the collection of
social security contributions; (b) strengthening the preventive health care
programs; (c) improving cost containment, management, and accounting of the
public health system; (d) rehabilitating public hospitals; and (e) developing
manuals on organization of maintenance systems and a computerized inventory
model for the health sector.
2.56      Chile's education sector is at a crucial development juncture.
Having now expanded the primary education system to attain virtual universal
coverage, the challenge ahead is to ensure equity in access to high quality
primary education while concurrently enhancing the internal efficiency of the
system. This will necessitate major changes in existing educational policies
and approaches, financing mechanisms, and administration and management.
Expansion of preschool opportunities for disadvantaged children is an
essential corollary to improve primary education performance. In addition,
the design of secondary education programs must be carefully reassessed and
modified, as necessary, to ensure that the vast majority of graduates for whom
this is the last stage of formal education can respond to rapidly evolving
labor market demands within a dynamic, modernizing economy. Bank involvement
in the education sector at this juncture can play an invaluable role, beyond
provision of investment financing ver se, in assisting the Government to
formulate appropriate policies, institute equitable financing schemes, and
design cost-effective educational programs which can contribute importantly to
attainment of Chile's overall development objectives. The proposed project,
the first in the Chilean educational sector since 1970Od to be financed by
the Bank, comprises an integral part of the Bank's overall country assist=nce
strategy to maintain and consolidate economic reforms, secure sustainable
economic growth and social development, and accelerate poverty alleviation.
The major focus of this will be on instituting cost-effective measures to
increase educational opportunities for low-income urban and rural children, a
problem which has been identified as a central concern in the Bank's recent
social sector work. Bank involvement assures continuation and consolidation
of economic and social reforms undertaken by the Chilean Government in the
past, and preservation of the decentralization of social services. This
project would complement sectoral financial resources already obtained through
the enactment of tax reforms. The Bank would provide valuable technical
iO/  IDB is preparing a project aimed at providing vocational training for
unemployed youth. The proposed IDB loan amounts to US$57.4 million.
Additionally, in the pipeline for FY92, IDB has another loan in the
amount of US$92.4 million to assist the Chilean Government in improving
higher education in science and technology.



- 19 -
assistance to transfer successful educational experiences from other
countries. It would also open dialogue about future Bank involvement in
secondary education, where complex curricular and financing issues await
policy attention.
2.57      The above policy is to be accomplished by instituting cost-effective
measures necessary to increase the efficiency of the public sector and
appropriate targeting and delivery of social programs to the poor. In the
education sector, the strategy is to help improve the educational opportuni-
ties available to children from low-income areas so they can participate more
actively and successfully in a rapidly evolving economy. Consistent with this
country and sector strategy, the Bank involvement in education investment aims
to support targeting public resources to the most vulnerable population groups
by: (a) extending the coverage and enhancing the quality of preschool educa-
tion; (b) enhancing the efficiency, quality, and equity of primary education;
(c) assessing the adequacy and responsiveness of secondaz-y education; and
(d) improving the institutional capacity of the Ministry of Education, the
regional and provincial educational departments, the municipalities, as well
as the managerial and pedagogical supervisory skills of municipal and private-
subsidized preschool and primary school principals. Sustaining the ongoing
decentralization process is an essential corollary to increase the efficiency,
quality, and equity of primary education.
2.58      Finally, the Bank involvement will help ensure greater sustainabi-
lity of programs, which are likely to become expensive as they expand if
economies of scale are not attained. The Bank will also help ensure the
program's cost-effectiveness and act as counterweight to excessive political
pressures for expansion beyond what can be justified.
2.59      Lessons Learned.  The most important lessons learned"' from
previous Bank experience in the primary education subsector are as follows:
(a) Interruptions or delays in provision of counterpart funds causes
implementation problems. If counterpart funds are not provided, or
are reduced, this has serious implications for the scope of the
project. Furthermore, project sustainability has been hampered by
inadequate allocation of resources and poor maintenance practices.
(b) The details of the project objectives and components need to be
clearly defined at the time of Board Approval to ensure that the
Borrower and the Bank are in agreement about the project definition
and scope, which should preclude implementation delays due to mis-
understanding or disagreement about project specifications.
11/ Obtained from an analysis of (i) 24 SARs, PCRs, and Audit Reports in LAC,
Asia and Africa; (ii) the recommendations of "Lending in Primary
Education" EDT #20, "Textbooks in the Developing World": EDI Seminar
Series and "Lending for Learning: Twenty Years of World Bank support for
Basic Education"; (iii) annual reviews of the education sector in the
Operations and Evaluation Department Annual Reviews from 1984 to 1988;
and (iv) "A World Bank Policy Paper: Primary Education."



- 20 -
(c) Before innovative reforms can be widely adopted, adequate pilot
testing is required to ensure that: (i) the new experience is in
harmony with the environment in which it is taking place; and
(ii) the best possible use is made of available resources. Also,
the overall success of education refoxrus depends on how well they
are implemented at the school level, thus the need to strengthen the
capacity of teaching and administrativ'e staff in both rural and
urban areas to carry out innovations.
(d) Primary education projects are inherently complex, since they
typically have several components, such as simultaneous construction
at several remote sites, introduction of innovations, and procure-
ment and delivery of educational materials in dispersed areas. This
requires an effective project management, monitoring, and supervi-
sion network. In addition, projects must be designed with flexibi-
lity so that they can easily adapt to changes in the country
situation.
(e) Direct purchasing is the best option for the procurement of
textbooks. Nevertheless, problems abound, including poor coordina-
tion of activities, poor quality of textbooks, distribution
failures, and inadequate teacher training in the use of improved
texts. Strategies to avoid these pitfalls focus on proper project
design, field testing of purchased books, close monitoring of the
distribution system, and a linkage between the teacher-training
program and textbook production %nd dissemmination.
(f)  Effective community participation has been instrumental in winning
popular support for educational (and other) reforms and in reducing
maintenance costs to the Government. However, community participa-
tion has been more effective in countries where such a tradition
already exists.
(g) Teacher training continues to be an important element of primary
education projects, but upgrading and continuity have been
neglected. Programs must include multigrade teacher training
programs and continuous training and upgrading.
2.60      These lessons have been incorporated into the design of the proposed
project.  Specifically, to ensure counternart funds, the Chilean Government
has already mobilized substantial additional resources for the education
sector through tax reform and national budget redistribution (para. 2.53). In
addition, provisions have been made to provide sufficient funds to meet
maintenance costs of school facilities. Furthermore, an agreement has been
reached with the Government for the Bank to comment the education budget
annually with the objective of ensuring that adequate resources are provided
to sustain the quality improvement program and for effective decentralization
(para 4.09). As for 2roiect scope, the Government's full involvement in
project design and preparation is a noteworthy aspect of the project.
Detailed operational manuals, procedures, and guidelines have been prepared
for the main project components and sub-components. With respect to the malor
innovation in this project, PRODEPE's pedagogical, technical, operational, and
managerial procedures are based on the already established "900 Deprived
Primary Schools Program" and the "Programa Nacional de Fomento al Desarrollo
de Innovaciones Educativas" (para. 2.52). To address the complexity issue,
classroom expaneion designs have been kept very simple, and the PIU will be
staffed by a small number of highly qualified and experienced personnel. The
PIU manager will report directly to



- 21 -
the Minister or Undersecretary level, and will be assisted in policy and
monitoring activities by a project executive council composed of senior
officials of the key MINEDUC departments involved in the implementation of the
project. Also, special arrangements for project supervision have been made
with the UNESCO regional office and local consultants. With respect to the
textbook component, textbooks will be produced and distributed by private
publishing houses in accordance with precise curricular and pedagogical
specifications in terms of reference prepared by MINEDUC. Enhancing current
comunity and garent particivation is another key element of this project. It
will be strengthened through offering specially designed workshops coupled
with a mass media campaign. PRODEPE will provide resources for quality
improvement projecto in selected municipal and private-subsidized schools. To
ensure proper implementation of this program, sDecial training courses for
both teachers and supervisors will be conducted to provide them with the
required skills.
III. THE PROJECT
3.01      The proposed Primary Education Improvement Project (Proyecto de
Mejoramiento de la Calidad y Equidad de la Educacion-MECE) responds to
educational sector development needs as identified in the Bank's recent social
sector work. The work concluded that: (a) improving educational
opportunities of children from low-income families is a high priority; (b)
extension of coverage and enhancement of the quality of preschool education is
crucial to any effort to improve the efficiency and outcome of primary
education; (c) improvements of the quality and equity of primary education
will contribute to reduce repetition and dropout rates and promote equitable
access, retention, and promotion rates, thus increasing internal primary
school efficiency by targeting appropriate interventions to low-income urban
and rural schools; and (d) revision of education subsidies could reduce
discrimination against small typically rural schools and subside pervasive
financial education deficits in municipal budgets.
A. Prolect Oblectives
3.02      The project's key objective is to assist the Government in enhancing
the efficiency, quality, and equity of primary education in selected schools
in rural and urban areas. To this end it will additionally: (a) expand
coverage and enhance the quality of preschool education to increase primary
school preparedness and reduce late entry, repetition, and dropout rates,
especially targeted to 5-year-old children living in poverty conditions; (b)
strengthen the institutional capacity of the MINEDUC, central, regional and
provincial offices, as well as that of the education departments of the
municipalities, and the managerial skills of municipal and private-subsidized
preschool and primary school administrators; and (c) assess alternative cost-
effective approaches to meet the secondary education needs of primary gradu-
ates.
B. Proiect Description
Enhancing the Efficiency. Ouality, and Eouitv of Primary Education
(US$180.7 million)
3.03      Efficiency, quality, and equity of primary education will be
enhanced by: (a) implementing 5,000 locally based quality improvement
projects of a pedagogical decentralization program (Programa de
Descentralizaci6n Pedag6gica--PRODEPE) through the participation of teachers



- 22 -
and principals of 5,000 selected municipal and private-subsidized primary
schools; (b) strengthening the educational testing system to assess and
monitor annually student performance for about 240,000 students from either
fourth or eighth grades every year; (c) providing inservice training for about
78,000 teachers, 8,000 subsidized primary school principals, and 625 supervi-
sors; (d) distributing, on a targeted cost-recovery scheme, about 24.5 million
primary textbooks for all the municipal and private subsidized primary
students, about 870,000 sets of complementary reading and teaching materials
for all primary subsidized schools, and teaching equipment for primary
schools; (e) hiring 500 new teachers to provide eight grades in 500 incomplete
rural primary schools; (f) constructing 500 classrooms in the rural areas; (g)
upgrading, and refurbishing classrooms in about 2,500, mostly rural primary
schools; (h) establishing an experimental education communication computer
network in 66 (mostly rural) primary schools; (i) providing health screening
and referral to the primary care system, as needed, to 250,000 first graders
every year; and (j) establishing monetary incentives for inservice teacher
training and teaching in deprived, mostly rural primary schools in the
framework of the new TS.
3.04      Pedagozical Decentralization Program (PRODEPE).  Like many
developing countries, before 1980 the tradition in Chile was a very central-
ized, top-down primary education style. The MINEDUC developed policies and
standards, oversaw all aspects of school aeministration, and provided all
financing from the center. In such an environment, local decision making was
limited, and local initiative was clearly not facilitated. The 1980 reform
presented a sharp break on this tradition. By moving operational control to
the municipalities and encouraging participation of the private sector, the
Government hoped to encourage greater local participation in primary education
development. With PRODEPE, the Government proposes a bold further step to
foster local initiative focused to solve key problems in quality improvement.
This program will be built from ground up, providing grant funding to locally
designed programs addressing the specific problems related to quality weak-
nesses at the school level. PRODEPE will: (a) enhance local curriculum in
reading, writing, mathematics, and science education through the participation
of teachers and school principals; and (b) establish and implement school
specific quality improvement projects in 5,000 municipal and private subsi-
dized primary schools.12 The PRODEPE will finance the implementation and
sustoinability of multi-year school quality improvement projects through the
participation of the PECs, and the municipal and provincial education authori-
12/ School quality improvement projects could consist, for example of:
(a) developing teaching and learning materials for primary school
students; (b) reinforcing teaching and learning of Spanish, mathematics,
natural and social sciences; (c) establishing new pedagogical models; (d)
creating school orchards and gardens; (e) implementing pedagogical and
supervisory teachers' workshops; (f) carrying out study programs
comprising visits to public and private institutions, attendance to
concerts and conferences, and other activities acceptable to the Bank for
primary school students and teachers; (g) organizing handicraft
activities among students; (h) implementing activities organized by
groups of parents and teachers and other associations; (i) organizing
periodic school exhibitions and fairs; (j) restructuring school
curricula; and (k) promoting school cultural activities.



- 23 -
ties. Resources for such projects will be made available on a competitive
basis assessed on the relative quantitative and qualitative merits of specific
proposals developed at the local level. Resources will go to selected and
targeted municipal and private subsidized schools that meet stated criteria
and present their innovative educational improvement projects in a timely
fashion. Details of PRODEPE implementation arrangements are in Annex 8.
3.05      Identifying those schools eligible for funding has been a focus of
project development. Six weighted criteria, explained in Annex 8, are used to
classify the municipal and private-subsidized primary schools into high,
medium,and low risk. During the project, PRODEPE will be targeted to 5,000
subsidized schools, comprising 62 percent of all municipal and private-
subsidized primary schools. Out of this universe, 2,300 schools will be high-
risk (100 percent of the total high-risk schools), 2,300 medium-risk (57 per-
cent of the total medium-risk schools), and 400 low risk (25 percent of the
total low-risk schools). This distribution clearly demonstrates an equity
focus of the project. The desirability to enrich PRODEPE with quality
improvement projects arising from good primary subsidized schools suggests the
inclusion of a small but relevant number of low-risk schools. The targeting
criteria to select participating schools will: (a) ensure poverty alleviation
impact by favoring high-risk, mostly poor, urban and rural subsidized primary
schools; and (b) insulate PRODEPE from political interference. The applica-
tion of suitable manuals approved during the appraisal, and procedures to rank
school quality improvement projects on their merit, will ensure enhancement of
the quality of education. Official approval by MINEDUC of PRODEPE's Opera-
tional Manual containing the implementation procedures, selection criteria and
the required proportion of school ranking was submitted to the Bank prior to
negotiations. It will be fo .ally adopted by MINEDUC as a condition of loan
effectiveness, and will be followed during project implementation. During
negotiations, agreements were reached that changes to the PRODEPE Manual that
materially and adversely affect the project would be considered an event of
default. Finally, the participation of teachers and school principals in the
design of quality improvement projects, of supervisors in their ranking, and
the municipal and provincial authorities, as well as the provincial education
Councils in their selection, will strengthen the decentralization process.
3.06      It is estimated that during the first year of the project (1992),
PRODEPE will finance up to 400 quality improvement projects (8 percent of the
targeted group of schools). For the following two years, PRODEPE will
increment the number of projects to be financed by 350. For the last three
years of the project, PRODEPE will finance an average of 1,000 projects per
year, reaching a total of 5,000 targeted subsidized schools during the project
life (see Annex 8). The gradual implementation of PRODEPE will allow for a
close and constant monitoring and supervision, and thus an orderly expansion
program. Criteria to set upper and lower bounds of the funds made available
to competing quality improvement projects, as well as the disbursement
characteristics including the school's counterpart resources, are explained in
Annex 8. Selected schools will be entitled to receive the improvement grant
only once during the project's lifespan.
3.07      After their preparation, school quality improvement projects
competing for financing must be endorsed by the school's principal. After
approval at the school level, projects will be presented to the provincial
education authorities (with PEC and municipal participation) for selection and
approval through the respective supervisory subsystem. Manuals to rank the



- 24 -
competing projects based on their quantitative and qualitative merits to
improve efficiency and quality of education will be tested and implemented
under the National Program to Foster Educational Innovations--currently being
implemented as a pilot program in about targeted 150 schools by the CPEIP
(paras. 2.52 and 3.08). The content of the agreement to be signed by MINEDUC
with the municipalities and private-subsidized school proprietors concerning
PRODEPE was discussed during the appraisal; the model contract to be signed
with each Municipality or private-subsidized school proprietors with terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank would be made a condition for loan
effectiveness. Agreements were reached during negotiations that an agreement
will be signed between MINEDUC and each municipality and private-subsidized
school proprietors where a project will be carried out, before approving the
respective project, substantially in the form of the model contract
satisfactory to the Bank. Under such agreements, the responsibilities and
obligation of the municipalities and schools are detailed in Annex 11, para.
8. All the key technical personnel involved in ranking, selecting, and
approving the quality improvement projects will be suitably trained. During
the entire life of the project: (a) 625 primary school supervisors will be
trained to rank quality improvement projects and assist teachers to partici-
pate in the pedagogical decentralization process; (b) 40 provincial two-person
technical teams will be annually retrained to upgrade their quality improve-
ment project selection, approval, and monitoring skills; (c) 8,000 primary
subsidized school principals and 8,000 teachers will be trained to upgrade
their quality improvement designing skills; and (d) 334 municipal and 490
private primary subsidized school proprietors will regularly be informed on
the progress of this program. MINEDUC will establish an information system to
register all competing quality improvement projects and to monitor the
implementation of the selected projects, and will implement an evaluation
study on PRODEPE's process and impact.
3.08      While an important innovation to Government policy, PRODEPE bases
its pedagogical, technical, financial, operational, and managerial procedures
on established Chilean experiences, such as the "900 Deprived Primary Schools
Program," implemented in 1990, and CPEIP's recently enacted "Programa Nacional
de Fomento al Desarrollo de Innovaciones Educativas", a pilot program targeted
to about 150 primary schools.
3.09      Pedagogical Supervision.  The project will finance the strengthening
of supervisory capacity at the primary schools and at MINEDUC's provincial
level respectively by: (a) providing technical-pedagogical training skills to
about 8,000 municipal and private subsidized primary school principals; and
(b) increaeing the number of primary education supervisors from the current
375 to 625, and providing them with training, supervisory Operational Manuals,
materials, and resources. During the project's life, CPEIP will coordinate
the design and implementation of 400 technical-pedagogical training skill
courses for school principals and 70 courses for primary education supervi-
sors, that will be provided by CPEIP and 34 higher learning institutions and
universities. During 1992 and 1993, approximately 55 training courses will be
offered annually, increasing to about 100 at the peak of the project in 1997.
3.10      In the future, supervisors will focus on technical-pedagogical
issues, leaving the administrative functions to the educational inspectors
assigned to the provincial offices of the MINEDUC. There will be one supervi-
sor for every 15 urban schools and one for every 13 rural schools. High-risk
schools will receive one pedagogical supervision visit per month. Prior to



- 25 -
negotiations, the Bank received the final version of the supervisory manual
which will be adopted by MINEDUC as a condition for loan effectiveness and
followed during project execution. During negotiations, agreements were
reached that changes to the Supervision Manual that materially and adversely
affect the project would be considered an event of default.
3.11      Academic Achievement Assessment.  SIMCE, the Achievement Assessment
System, will be strengthened to ensure that: (a) all tests used to measure
student learning achievement meet stringent psychometric standards of
reliability and validity; (b) information gathering activities are carried out
in a timely and efficient manner; (c) results reporting will be easily
interpretable and will facilitate improving instructional practice at the
school level and policy decisions concerning the efficient use of school
resources; and (d) results analysis will be dove by competent research
specialist to provide feedback to school authorities on educational and social
significance of the assessment findings.
3.12      During the project's life, SIMCE will be expanded to also measure
the academic achievement in small, mostly rural, incomplete and/or multigrade
primary school.s with six or less fourth and eighth graders, comprising a
universe of 34,000 students. A secondary school achievement evaluation system
(SIMCE-Media) targeted for 130,00C secondary sophomores (para. 3.54) will be
gradually implemented. The project will finance the required hardware,
peripheral equipment, personnel, training, technical assistance, license,
inservice training and studies required in the assessment system.
3.13      The Chilean educational authorities have transferred SIMCE's
technological capacity, including the intellectual leadership, from the UC to
the MINEDUC. This decision has been taken on the ground of MINEDUC's desire
to build its own assessment capacity, to reduce costs by using the same
equipment of the EIS in primary and secondary schools' achievement evaluation,
and timely availability of the scores information. The SIMCE group will be
directly accountable to the Minister and the Undersecretary on staff
organizational and institutional capacity. SIMCE will continue being imple-
mented on a fourth and eig:^th grader census basis, mainly because it is an
effective feedback information device to induce school teachers and principals
to participate in the quality improvement program. Continuous training will
be provided to SIMCE's technical and support staff. SIMCE results are
expected to be periodically disseminated among MINEDUC and municipal
educational authorities, school principals and teachers, interested NGOs,
research centers, and parents.
3.14      Inservice Teacher Training and Upgradinz.  The inservice teacher
training will be coordinated by PIU and CPEIP, and implemented through CPEIP'S
central and regional offices, as well as with the participation of 34 other
higher learning institutions and universities throughout the country. The
project will finance the following activities:  (a) reinforcing CPEIP to
design and produce inservice teaching materials; (b) producing video, audio,
and printed teaching materials for distance inservice activities; (c) estab-
lishing 330 communal inservice teaching training workshop networks to
strengthen the distance inservice teaching training program; (d) providing
distance inservice training to 63,000 primary teachers; and (e) training for
14,600 regular teachers, out of whom 8,400 teachers will be trained in early
detection of learning disabilities to facilitate timely referral of students



- 26 -
in need uf differentiated education, and 6,200 (mostly) teachers from
multigrade and/or incomplete rural schools will be trained in quality
improvement project formulation to induce their participation in PRODEPE.
3.15      The distance inservice teaching training aims to help teachers to
design quality improvement projects, for use in their daily professional
practice. The program is intended for approximately 6,300 teachers per year
duri-lg the first two years of the project's life, increasing the number of
participants to almost 19,000 by the last two years. Periodic monitoring and
supervision of this program will be provided by 16,000, previously trained
school. principals and teachers in 8,000 short-term workshops held during the
entire life of the project, comprising 8 to 10 teachers per workshop.13'  The
training of teachers for detection of learning disabilities and timely
referral of these students will be provided in 210, one week, in-class courses
during the entire life of the project.
3.16      Textbooks and Other Education Inputs.  Educational 'nputs such as
textbooks, reading materials, teaching guides, and educatioral technology will
be targeted mainly to deprived urban and rural schools. The MINEDUC will
gradually update the textbooks of mathematics, Spanish, history, geography and
natural sciences, procuring them from private publishers. The books corres-
ponding to the first, second, fifth and sixth grades of primary education will
be updated during 1992, while the ones corresponding to the other grades will
be reformulated during 1993. Textbooks intended for rural areas will be
supplemented with additional relevant teaching materials. A total of about
24.5 million books (approximately 2 million for the rural areas) will be
produced and distributed by the contracted publishing houses during the
lifespan of the project. Textbook updating will be executed according to the
MINEDUC's precise curricular and pedagogical terms of reference. A teaching
guide for each subject will be published and distributed to every primary
school teacher. First to fourth grade textbooks, used simultaneously for
teaching and student written exercises, will have a one-year lifespan during
the entire project, whereas fifth to eighth grade textbooks will have a two-
year lifespan. The new textbooks will provide the national curriculum
required by law; regional and local teaching materials would be incorporated
into the curricula by interested schools through PRODEPE. Experience gained
in the pilot bilingual and rural program of the University of La Frontera,
Temuco, will be transferred to design and produce rural complementary teaching
materials for ethnic students. Private publishers bidding for textbooks will,
in coordination with CPEIP. provide the necessary teacher training in the use
of these materials.
3.17       A targeted cost-recovery policy directed to subsidized primary
schools located in above medium-income areas will be implemented. About
20,000 primary students from high income subsidized schools, and 380,000 from
medium- to high-income subsidized primary schools will be required to purchase
the textbooks at subsidized prices. This cost-recovery policy will generate
about US$250,000 per year. During negotiations, the Bank obtained assurances
that the Government will enact this policy, which will be a condition to
13/ During the peak of the distance in-service teacher training in 1995 and
