per month.



- 27 -
commence disbursements of the primary textbook subcomponent. The average
textbook unit cost is US$0.92; without the small editions of especially
designed rural textbooks, the average unit cost decreases to about US$0.80. A
research study will be completed to ascertain the cost-effectiveness of the
one- and two-year use of textbooks.
3.18      The in class reading resource center program (Rincones de Lectura)
is designed to induce reading habits among first and second graders, their
teachers and parents. This program will supply 80 books (sets of 35 books for
every first-grade classroom, and 45 for every second grade classroom) of each
subsidized primary school, during the lifespan of the project. The process to
select the appropriate subject matter incorporated in the reading resource
centers to be procured from private publishers will be derived from the
ongoing experience of the "900 Deprived Primary Schools Program." Starting in
1993, the third and fourth grade classrooms of all subsidized primary schools
will receive sets of 60 books each. This project will finance in aggregate
24,500 sets of books.
3.19      The project will also finance the annual production of 23,000
textbooks to develop basic (written, oral, arithmetic, senso-
perceptive, and psychcmotor) functions of primary students with learning
disabilities, and 800,000 sets of specially designed Spanish and mathematics
learning materials for first to fourth graders in all subsidized primary
schools.
3.20      Supplementing PRODEPE, the project will finance the procurement of
about 5,OCO televisions and videocassettes, 30,000 videotapes, 10,000 tape
recorders, 100,000 audio cassettes, and 5,000 basic copying machines, which
will be made available to quality improvement projects requiring some of the
above education inputs. Concurrently, a teacher training program on how to
use this equipment will be provided by CPEIP. The production of video and
audio educational materials will be contracted with different public and
private academic and research centers, including CPEIP. The education
technological equipment will be procured in packages accordingly to the
demands registered in the revision of competing quality improvement projects.
3.21      The project will finance the enhancement of the teaching and
learning process in 3,700 high-risk, mostly rural (multigrade and/or
incomplete) primary schools by: (a) providing one encyclopedia per school;
and (b) furnishing one teaching manual and three teaching guides (one for
math, Spanish and science) for each of the 6,200 teachers assigned to these
schools. Furthermore, pedagogical innovations, based on the ongoing pilot
bilingual and rural program of the University of La Frontera, Temuco, intended
for incomplete and/or multigrade schools, will be tested beforehand in five to
ten selected rural schools throughout the country (to be labeled "Testing
Ground Schools") prior to general implementation.
3.22      Hiring Teachers and Constructing Classrooms for Subsidized
Incomplete Primary Schools. To allow poor, mostly rural sixth graders to
complete their primary eighth-grade cycle, the project will finance, on a
declining basis, the hiring of 500 new teachers to an equal number of
subsidized primary high-risk schools. The 500 primary high-risk incomplete
schools with less than sixth graders, out of a universe of 3,814 incomplete
schools, will be selected. Newly appointed teachers will share the teaching



- 28 -
load among all school enrollers, so as to seek a more uniform teaching
distribution and reduce the per pupil teacher ratio. Finally, 500 classrooms
will be constructed among the selected schools.
3.23      Remodelling of Primary Schools.  The project will finance the
remodelli.ng of about 2,500, mostly deprived urban and rural primary schools,
with the participation of the school community and the municipalities. School
remodeling projects will be selected on the basis of their cost-effectiveness,
degree of local participation, and municipal commitment for sustaining
maintenance. The technical, financial disbursement, managerial, and
logistical learning experience achieved in school remodelling in the "900
Deprived Primary Schools Program" will be transferred to this project. The
content of the agreement to be reached by MINEDUC with the Municipalities for
remodelling primary schools was provided during the appraisal; the model
contract to be signed with each municipality with terms and conditions
satisfactory to the Bank will be made condition for loan effectiveness.
Agreements were reached during negotiations that an agreement will be signed
between MINEDUC and each municipality where a school's remodelling be carried
out before disbursing any funds to such municipality, substantially in the
form of the model contract satisfactory to the Bank. Under such agreement,
the responsibilities and obligations of the municipalities are in Annex 11,
para. 9.
3.24      Computer Network.  An experimental educational computer network to
promote the participation of teachers and students in designing relevant
regional teaching materials in the four primary education subject areas
(Spanish, mathematics, science, and social studies) will be launched in 66
(mostly rural) primary schools in 22 communities in three adjacent regions of
the country.  This program is based on the followjing six criteria:
(a) computers will be used as means and never as pedagogical ends; (b) the
computer network will be geared to reinforce the extracurricular activities of
participating schools; (c) the network activities' design will be linked to
the local and regional realities of the users; (d) an interdisciplinary
approach, linking different subject areas, will be sought; (e) individual
capability and creativity will be fostered; and (f) computer-aided prepackaged
educational software will not be allowed in the network.14 The project will
finance local consultants, hardware, software development, and training.
3.25      As a complement to the above experimental project, an evaluation
study on the impact of computers in the Chilean primary education system has
been included in the project. Terms of reference for such study to be
implemented by the CPEIP have been set. The results of both this diagnosis
and the experimental computer network will guide future sectoral policies on
this complex issue. The project will finance local consultants trained in the
use of computers in education and evaluation.
3.26      Health Care Assistance.  A yearly screening, referral, and limited
assistance health care program for 250,000 first-grade primary students of all
municipal and private-subsidized schools will be implemented with the partici-
pation of the local health authorities. This program aims to perform yearly
14/ Commercial communication software will be procured from private
suppliers.



- 29 -
diagnosis of first-grade primary school students concerning: nutritional
status; auditory and visual acuity; genital, spinal, foot and dermatological
problems; and parasitosis.s' Students in need of medical assistance will be
referred to the proper health authorities. A finished school health manual
has been implemented. The project will finance: (a) the printing manuals for
12,000 primary school teachers in charge of health programs; (b) 40 yearly
inservice training courses for teachers serving as facilitators of this
program; (c) 44 staffyears to supervise this program; and (d) the screening
services provided for a fee by the municipal health authorities and private
clinics.
3.27      Professional TS.  A TS has been approved by the Chilean Congress
(Law 19070, dated July 1, 1991) including the requisite financial resources
required for its implementation. This TS seeks to improve teachers
productivity by: (a) raising the minimum professional salary of a primary
school teacher to 57,000 pesos (US$165 equivalent) per month for 44 weekly
chronological hours (33 weekly lecturing hours and 11 cocurricular hours); (b)
regulating the out-of-class pedagogical activities that teachers are paid for
and expected to perform; (c) inducing inservice teacher training from which
teachers will be promoted on a selective, quality-control merit basis; and (d)
motivating skilled and experienced teachers to serve in deprived urban and
rural schools. On the cost side, the TS is expected to have the additional
costs detailed in para. 2.53 and in Table 1, Annex 5. The project will
finance on a declining basis the following aspects of the TS: (a) the
inservice teacher training costs for about 78,000 municipal and private subsi-
dized preschool and primary school teachers and principals participating in
the project; and (b) incentives for teaching in about 3,700 deprived urban and
rural primary schools. During negotiations, an agreement was reached that
changes to the TS Law (Law 19070 dated July 1, 1991) that materially and
adversely affect the project would be considered an event of default.
3.28      The TS implementation runs the risk of granting automatic salary
increases for inservice teacher training based on attendance instead of
selective and quality assessment criteria upon completion of course. This
situation is compounded by the salary increases granted by the TS based on
seniority. To minimize these risks, luring negotiations, Government provided
assurances that will put in place no later than June 30, 1992 a system whereby
the entire teaching force will be encouraged to participate in periodic
upgrading inservice teaching training during the life of the project. For
loan effectiveness, the Bank would require copies of Government Bylaws
regulating inservice teacher training certification based on selectivit- and
quality control merits be enacted.
3.29      Concurrently with the TS, the Executive Branch of the Chilean
Government has submitted to Congress an initiative to amend the SL. This
initiative aims to: (a) introduce equity into the system by increasing the
subsidy for adult education, differentiated education, and by raising the
resources made available to small, mostly, rural schools with a total
enrollment of less than 100 students; and (b) modify the monthly average
15/ The Chilean municipal health authorities and private clinics will
provide, for a fee per student, the screening for parasitosis, nutrition-
al status, dermatological problems, and visual acuity.



- 30 -
attendance subsidy formula to a longer period, to reduce present significant
monthly changes in revenues to school, contributing to large education
deficits in many municipal budgets. During negotiations, the Bank obtained
assurances that annual budgets allocate each year of project implementation
the specific minimum amounts required to finance the SL as follows: $2.0
million equivalent for 1992; $2.7 million equivalent for 1993; $3.2 million
equivalent for 1994; $3.4 million equivalent for 1995; and thereafter. The
first yearly (1992) allocation is to be provided as a condition of
effectiveness.
3.30      Complementary to the TS and the modifications to the SL, the Chilean
education authorities have increased the schoolyear from 36 to 37 weeks,
effective last March, when the 1991 schoolyear began. This increase will
undoubtedly contribute to achieving the project's objectives as more time on
task is spent by teachers with their students.
Expanding the Coverage and Enhancing the Quality of Preschool Education
Targeted to Poor Children (US$39.3 million)
3.31      The principal objectives of the preschool education component would
be to assist the Government in increasing the achievement levels of deprived
children to first grade expanding access to preschool education of poor
children. The project also would increase the quality of targeted existing
preschool services, thus contributing to: (a) raising the readiness levels
required for performing in first grade at expected "standard" levels;
(b) reducing late entry and facilitate the timely graduation from primary
education of deprived students; (c) reducing functional illiteracy by raising
performance in primary education; and (d) helping working women by taking care
of their young children.
3.32      The project will finance preschool expansion for about 16,000 urban
and 30,000 rural, mostly 5-year-old poor children, by: (a) hiring teachers,
paraprofessionals, and supervisors; (b) creating new facilities and school
additions; and (c) expanding existing services. The project. would also
finance the enhancement of targeted ongoing preschool serv'oes. Finally, the
project will finance an evaluation process to study the cost-effec_iveness of
each urban and rural preschool alternative and its impact on the future
academic achievement, behavior, and skills of the children.
3.33      The urban and rural preschool models to be implemented would be
cost-effective, replicable, and sustainable. Preschool poverty alleviation
impact will be attained by: (a) targeting the urban expansion in poor areas
whose primary students have scored low in SIMCE, show late primary entrance,
high repetition and dropout rates, as well as, below average primary comple-
tion rates; and (b) primarily increasing the preschool supply in rural areas.
3.34      Preschool Expansion.  The project will finance the expansion of
preschool education for about 16,000 urban 5-year-old children by: (a) hiring
140 preschool municipal teachers; (b) constructing 100 municipal classrooms
providing about 4,500 spaces; (c) hiring and training 490 paraprofessionals
for the INTEGRA and JUNJI options and using 7,000 spaces of unused capacity in
centers currently operated by INTEGRA as well as 4,000 unused spaces of the
ongoing JUNJI program; (d) developing and distributing 11,300 sets of teaching
materials; and (e) implementing a school feeding program to provide 16,000
daily rations to deprived preschoolers. The school feeding program is



- 31 -
administered by the National School Assistance and Scholarship Board (Junta
Nationale de Auxilio Escolar y Becas--JUNAEB), which procures the distribu-
tion, storage, and preparation, with eligible competing bidders from the
private sector.
3.35      Preschool education in rural areas for about 30,000 new children
under 6 years old would be provided through two models. Both alternatives
will provide: (a) about 29,000 sets of teaching materials; and (b) continuous
inservice teaching training for supervisors, school principals, teachers, and
paraprofessionals. In addition, the first program involves the active parti-
cipation of parents in program implementation, training of about 23,400
parents in 3,000 local workshops, which would be organized and implemented by
about 43 existing and 8 newly hired preschool supervisors and 615 trained
paraprofessionals. This alternative will not provide food supplements to the
participants. The second program, to be implemented by INTEGRA, comprises:
(a) hiring 15 supervisors and 150 paraprofessionals; (b) refurbishing 75 small
rural centers donated by the respective communities, each one holding about 25
children under 6 years old each, and being operated by two paraprofessionals;
and (c) providing 2,000 daily food rations.
3.36      Construction of about 100 new municipal preschool classrooms would
be targeted to low-income urban or urban-rural communities with: (a) more
than 30,000 inhabitants and a population of 5-year-olds of 300 or more lacking
preschool education; and (b) primary schools that have scored low on SIMCE and
have late entrance, repetition, and dropout rates above the national average.
Classroom construction will be awarded to private local contractors on a
shopping or direct contracting basis, as detailed in para. 5.06. Munici-
palities will be held accountable for awarding the contracts and supervising
the execution of the construction projects, as well as for the systematic and
periodic maintenance of the school premises. The classroom will operate, when
possible, in double shifts serving approximately 32 children per shift.
INTEGRA will be accountable for procuring the services to adapt 75 local
facilities donated by the respective rural communities to implement the small
rural centers program. The plans and estimated costs of both the municipal
and INTEGRA models are incorporated in the project working papers. The draft
agreement to be reached by MINEDUC with the Municipalities for construction
preschools construction was provided during the appraisal. The model
contract, with terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank, will be made
condition for loan effectiveness. Agreement was reached during negotiations
that an agreement will be signed between MINEDUC and each municipality where
didactic material be provided; additional teachers be financed; school feeding
program be expanded; and a classroom construction be carried out before
disbursing any funds to such municipality, substantially in the form of the
model contract satisfactory to the Bank. Under such agreement, the responsi-
bilities and obligations of the municipalities are in Annex 11, para. 10.
3.37      Enhancing Preschool Quality.  The project will finance the enhance-
ment of existing preschool quality services provided by the municipalities,
JUNJI, INTEGRA, and the private subsidized proprietors, especially in poor
urban and rural areas by: (a) reinforcing preschool supervision and teaching;
(b) designing, producing, and distributing teaching materials; (c) inducing
parent participation in the early stimulation of their children; and (d)
implementing an evaluation study to measure: (i) cost-effectiveness of the
alternatives used by the project; and (ii) the medium-term impact on
children's academic achievement, behavior, and skills.



- 32 -
3.38      The project will finance, under a declining basis for recurrent
costs, the strengthening of the preschool supervision and teaching by:
(a) hiring 50 new supervisors (7 for MINEDUC's urban program and 43 for
JUNJI); (b) hiring 163 preschool teachers and 164 paraprofessionals; and
(c) implementing suitable technical-pedagogical supervisory inservice training
courses for about 200 supervisors, 3,300 school principals and teachers, and
2,700 paraprofessionals of all agencies involved. The technical-pedagogical
ad-hoc committee (comprised of the MINEDUC, JUNJI, INTEGRA, and the CPEIP) are
in the process of formulating the appropriate inservice training courses
content, which will be executed by CPEIP and/or various higher learning
institutions and universities.
3.39      The project will finance, under a targeted cost-recovery policy,
the production and distribution of 102,000 sets of preschool didactic
materials especially designed for the different operating urban and rural
modalities. All subsidized preschools located in above-medium-income urban
areas will be required to purchase these teaching materials at subsidized
prices. Preliminary figures estimate that the cost-recovery policy will
generate about US$200,000 during the project lifespan. The teaching materials
will be designed to foster gross and fine coordination skills among preschool
students, as well as psychomotor and social skills. Complementary training
and teaching materials will be designed to induce parent participation in pre-
school activities in the rural areas. A technical-pedagogical committee
comprised by the MINEDUC, JUNJI, INTEGRA, and CPEIP will provide the terms of
reference of the teaching materials, whose production and distribution will be
procured from the private sector. An agreement was reached during
negotiations that regulations concerning the cost-recovery policy will be put
I         into effect prior to commencing disbursements of the preschool teaching
material subcomponent.
3.40      The project will finance parent involvement in the early stimula-
tlon and education of their children by: (a) implementing a rural, tested
prcschool model that calls for the participation of approximately 23,400,
previously trained (in 3,000 specially designed workshops) parents in the
delivery of preschool education; and (b) instituting a mass media campaign on
early childhood stimulation directed to parents and consisting of 100 short
radio and TV messages, 20 spots, 24 miniseries, and assorted printed material.
3.41      Evaluation Studiec.  The project will also finance:  (a) an
evaluation study of preschool education impact on children's further academic
achievement, behavior, and skills; and (b) a cost-effectiveness study
comparAng the three urban and two rural preschool models. The evaluation will
attempt to appraise and compare the relevance, efficiency, and effectiveness
of each preschool alternative financed under this project. In the medium
term, a random sample of children of each preschool modality will be monitored
and followed throughout their further education, to attempt to assess
cognitive achievement, psychomotor and social skills development. A control
group of children with no preschool education will also be selected,
monitored, and followed. The results of the cost-effectiveness studies will
guide future government preschool expansion policies. The terms of reference
of such studies were approved during appraisal.



- 33 -
3.42      The content of the agreements to be signed by MINEDUC with the
municipalities, INTEGRA and JUNJI to provide didactic material; to hire
additional teachers, supervisors and paraprofessionals; to expand the school
feeding program; to construct additional classrooms; and to refurbish and
equip small rural centers was discussed during the appraisal mission. Tho
model contract to be signed with each municipality with terms and conditionis
satisfactory to the Bank would be made a condition for loan effectiveness.
Agreement was reached during negotiations that agreements will be signed
between MINEDUC, each municipality, INTEGRA and JUNJI, where the activities
described above will be carried out, before commencing implementation of the
respective part, substantially in the form of the model contract satisfactory
to the Bank. Responsibilities and obligations of the municipalities, TNTEGRA
and JUNJI under such agreements are in Annex 11, para. 11.
Strengthening the Institutional. Financial, and Managerial Capacitv of
MINEDUC, the Provinces, and the Municipalities (US$16.5 million)
3.43      The institutional capacity of the MINEDUC, its regional and
provincial educational departments, and the municipalities will be
strengthened by providing: (a) the MINEDUC with timely and relevant
information and procedures to translate effectively educational policies and
strategies into concrete programmatic actions, target resources to high-risk
schools through the municipalities, and periodically monitor sector achieve-
ments; (b) municipalities with procedures, guidelines, and training to execute
effectively educational programs derived from sector policies, and administer
efficiently the resources of their assigned municipal schools; and (c) school
administrators with managerial and technical-pedagogical supervision skills.
The ongoing decentralization process will be further supported by: (a)
enhancing the managerial and technical skills of appropriate staff of MINEDUC,
the regional and provincial education departments, as well as the municipali-
ties through training programs oriented to induce a more efficient and effec-
tive deployment and targeting of available resources; and (b) establishing an
EIS.
3.44      Strengthening the MINEDUC and the Municipal Managerial Capacities.
The project will finance the development of procedures and operational
manuals, appropriate skill training courses, relevant techniques for in-depth
analysis of the EIS, and supply of equipment (computers, printers and
peripheral equipment, facsimiles, vehicles) especially tailored for the
different levels of decision-making that conform to the Chilean education
system.
3.45      At the MINEDUC central and regional levels (SEREMIS) the project
aims to strengthen the: (a) sectoral planning, programming, budgeting,
resource disbursement, monitoring, and evaluation processes; (b) purchasing
systems; and (c) administration of personnel. At MINEDUC's provincial level,
the project seeks to strengthen the: (a) role of the provincial authorities
in PRODEPE; (b) preschool and primary pedagogical supervision systems; and
(c) administrative inspection bodies to monitor the correct application of the
SL. Prior to negotiations, the Bank received the final version of the
administrative inspection manual, to be applied by the MINEDUC Provincial
Secretariat inspectors, which will be formally adopted as a condition for loan
effectiveness. During negotiations, an agreement was reached that changes to
the Inspection Manual that materially and adversely affect the project would
be considered an event of default.



- 34 -
3.46      Within the Municipal Education Departments (280) and Municipal
Corporaciones (54) the project attempts to strengthen: (a) municipal
participation in PRODEPE; (b) educational micro-planning procedures for
efficient deployment and targeting of available resources; (c) personnel
Administration; (d) formulation of school construc-tion, equipping, and
refurbishing projects; and (e) financing, accounting, and auditing. One of
the immediate expected byproducts of this participatory process is to deploy
better available resources, thus increasing the efficiency and effectiveness
of the education system, and gradually reducing the overstaffing situation
that may exist in selected municipal primary schools.
3.47      At the school level the project aims to reinforce:  (a) municipal
and private subsidized proprietors awareness to MECE's objectives and periodic
improvements; and (b) the education management skills of municipal and
private-subsidized preschool and primary school principals.
3.48      Training Programs and Manuals.  To reinforce MINEDUC's central,
regional, and provincial administration as well as municipal managerial skills
and procedures the project will finance: (a) training for about 500 middle
management staff to improve their planning, programming, budgeting, and
monitoring capacities; (b) management training for 334 municipal educational
authorities (280 municipal departments of education, and 54 municipal
Corporaciones); (c) providing management skill training for about 1,200
preschool and primary school administrators; and (d) developing practical
manuals for planning, programming, administration, budgeting and monitoring
decentralized education activities. The execution of the courses will be
procured through local (higher learning institutions, universities, and the
private sector) and external technical assistance (consultants).
3.49        Educational Information System (EIS).  An EIS will be established,
linking the following ongoing information subsystems: (a) SIMCE; (b) educa-
tional statistics; (c) programming and budgeting statistics; (d) budget
disbursements; and (e) MINEDUC's administration and accounting. During the
life of the project, the EIS will be expected to include: (a) a quality
improvement project implementation monitoring information subsystem; (b) an
educational research inventory subsystem (SINIE); (c) an education physical
plant inventory subsystem; and (d) a MECE monitoring and impact evaluation
information subsystem. The project will finance the design of the EIS, the
development and purchasing of appropriate software and hardware, the
administrative support systems, the information and dissemination process, and
the required local and external technical assistance. The EIS will require 2
mainframes at MINEDUC's central level and 197 microcomputers (18 at MINEDUC
central, 45 in the Seremis, 121 at the provincial offices, and 13 at the
CPEIP).
3.50        In addition to the preschool evaluation study (para. 3.41), the
project would also finance process and impact evaluation of the following
subcomponents: (a) PRODEPE; (b) pedagogical supervision; (c) inservice
teacher training; (d) textbooks and reading materials; (e) specific inter-
ventions aimed at rural and bilingual education; (f) communication computer
network; (g) TS and the modification of the monthly average student attendance
in the subsidy formula; (h) EIS; (i) MINEDUC and the municipal maaagerial
capacities; and (j) other basic education innovations implemented by NGOs.
Assessing Alternative Cost-Effective Strategies to Meet the Needs of Primary
Education Graduates (US$2.6 million)



- 35 -
3.51        This component proposes to gather information, including policy-
oriented research, focusing on the status of secondary education, its costs
and effectiveness. Studies will explore possible alternatives that Chile
might pursue to meet the needs of a rapidly growing number of primary school
graduates, many of whom seek access to secondary school. In connection with
this information-gathering effort, the project would include an information
and communications campaign with the aim of raising public consciousness
regarding subsector issues, listening to the views of concerned parties, and
generating consensus on policies and actions that need to be taken. An
additional aim of this component will be to provide the basis for strategic
decisions on policy for secondary education as well as for preparation of a
possible second education loan to improve coverage, efficiency, quality and
equity of the subsector.
3.52        The component includes information-gathering, public consultation
activities and studies regarding: (a) the present curriculum, the process of
curriculum development, teacher training and evaluation; (b) the internal
functioning of secondary schools; and (c) economic factors affecting secondary
education and its several streams and modalities. The terms of reference of
such studies have been prepared and reviewed by the Bank, and have been
incorporated in the project's working documents. The studies will be carried
out by selected higher learning institutions, universities, and research
centers. The administration of the studies will be carried out by the CPEIP.
Technical local and external assistance would be requested if deemed necessary
to supervise particular studies.
3.53        Curriculum Development, Teacher Training and Evaluation.  The
project will finance the following studies relating to the present curriculum
and the process of curriculum development and evaluation: (a) analysis of
current practices in curriculum development; (b) development of a proposal for
curriculum change; (c) exploration of possible interactions between schools
and their surrounding communities and pr4vate enterprises in decentralized
curriculum development and adaptation; (d) preservice teacher training; and
(e) assessment of the skills and abilities that students will need to function
effectively in Chile's modern society and labor market.
3.54        Internal Functioning of Secondary Schools.  The project will
finance the following studies relating to the internal functioning of
secondary schools: (a) work practices within the secondary subsector;
(b) development of valid prototypes for an improved system of supervision for
secondary education; (c) development of an achievement assessment system
(SIMCE-Media, para. 3.12) to be applied to 130,000 sophomores; and
(d) development of an adequate system to aid students in planning their own
education and careers.
3.55        Studies of Economic Factors.  The project will finance the follow-
Ing studies of economic factors affecting secondary education and its several
streams and modalities: (a) an evaluation of secondary education finance and
management; (b) a detailed analysis of costs by type and stream; (c) a study
of the external efficiency and economic returns to secondary education, with



- 36 -
special emphacis on the technical-vocational stream; and (d) costing of the
instructional needs of a revised prototype of secondary education, including
simulations of future expenditures and financing.
3.56        Information and Communication Campaign.  The project will finance
an information and a communication campaign to be carried out at the same time
these studies are in progress. Information from the studies will form a part
of the content of the campaign, while feedback from interested stake-holders
will influence the orientation and emphasis of the studies. Since the purpose
of the campaign is to raise public consciousness, generate constructive debate
and form the basis for secondary education policies, the findings of the
studies will enter directly into the deliberations regarding national policy
and strategy and help provide accurate information to guide the debate.
IV. PROJECT COST AND FINANCING PLAN
A. Cost of the Prolect
4.01        The total cost of the project is estimated at US$243.0 million
based on estimated prices of May 1991. The total foreign exchange cost is
estimated at US$34.2 million, or 14 percent of the total cost. Table 4.1
summarizes the estimated project costs by component and main intervention
categories. Detailed project costs are shown in Annex 9.
Contingency Allowance
4.02      Physical contingencies amount to 10 percent of base costs for civil
works, and 5 percent of base costs for goods and equipment for all components.
No physical contingencies are considered for consultant services. Price
contingencies for foreign costs are based on a projected international
inflation rate of 5 percent for 1991, 1.8 percent for 1992, 1.9 percent for
1993, 3.9 percent for 1994, 4.9 percent for 1995, 4.2 percent for 1996, and
3.6 percent for 1997. Local cost price contingencies are based on a local
projected inflation of 18.8 percent for 1991, 17.2 percent for 1992,
19.7 percent for 1993, 21.7 percent for 1994, 21.1 percent for 1995,
20.5 percent for 1996, and 19.8 percent for 1997.
Duties and Taxes
4.03      Locally procured goods are subject to a value-added tax (VAT) of 18
percent. Imported goods such as computers and other office technology
equipment are subject to 14 percent of customs tax and 18 percent of VAT. The
estimated base cost of the project includes local taxes, which amount to
approximately US$14.0 million.
B. Financing Plan
4.04      The proposed project would be financed by a Bank loan of
US$170.0 million or 74 percent of total project cost net of duties and taxes.
It would cover 100 percent of foreign exchange expenditures and 65 percent of
the estimated local costs. The government of Chile would finance the
remaining US$73.0 million including duties and taxes. Table 4.2 gives the
project financing plan, with a breakdown by project categories.

C. Education Sector Budxet for FY91 and Beyond
4.05        Due to increases in public revenues resulting from the recent
reform of the tax law, government will have the fiscal capability to sustain
the project. The first effects of the tax law were felt at the end of FY90,
how-ever, the first full year in which the law will be in effect is 1991.
From 1991 onward the Ministry of Hacienda has provided for a 12 percent
increase in social spending and, within the social sectors, the increase
allowed in education and health is 15 percent, reflecting the priority
accorded these sectors.
4.06        The Budget Law for 1991 provides 255.6 billion pesos for the
Ministry of Education (US$740.5 million). An additional allocation of 10.2
billion pesos (US$29.6 million) appears as a public treasury budget item to
cover the expected costs of implementing the TS during 1991, bringing the
total to 265.7 billion pesos (US$770.1 million). The substantial increase of
27 percent between the authorized budgets for FY90 and FY91 (32 percent with
the TS budget included) reflects the impact of the tax law and not a long-term
trend. The government recognizes that sector recurrent spending can only grow
in proportion with overall growth of public revenues and spending, and that
spending within the sector has to be carefully targeted to accomplish its
quality and equity objectives within budgetary constraints. The proposed
project, with its emphasis on improving quality, targeting spending on schools
serving low-income communities, and enhancing sector efficiency is fully



- 39 -
consistent with sectoral spending targets and will take place within a context
of public spending levels and policy that are far less restrictive than in
other countries in the region and are favorable to social sector spending,
especially for education and health.
D. Recurrent Costs Arising from the Project
4.07        The project is dominated by noninfrastructure expenditures for
teacher training; textbooks; educational materials and supplies; techn-J.eal
assistance and training; school feeding and health services; policy analyses;
evaluation, monitoring, and supervision. Some of the costs resulting from the
project will be continued after project completion and will become part of the
sector's ongoing recurrent costs. Indications that the project is being
sustained after completion will include continuing expenditures for inservice
teacher training, pedagogical supervision and monitoring, continued supply of
textbooks and teaching materials and supplies, achievement testing, school
feeding and health screening targeted to children of poor families, and
continued maintenance and physical improvement of facilities serving children
in poor communities. The proposed Bank loan would finance recurrent costa of
the project on a declining basis, from approximately 50 percent in the first
year of implementation (1992) to zero percent in the last year (1997). The
bulk of the recurrent cost to be financed by the Government will occur in
1996, representing US$16.9 million, which is less than one percent of the
sector budget. The increase in annual recurrent expenditures that will
continue after the project is completed, including the part of the TS directly
linked to the proposed project, has been estimated at US$22.1 million (7,625
billion pesos) (Table 4.3 below and Annex 5, Table 1). This will represent
1.8 percent of the estimated sector expenditure in the first year after the
project is completed (1998).
E. Prolect Sustainabilitv
4.08        The effect of the proposed project on total sector costs anid the
ability of the government to sustain the expenditures that will result from
full project implementation are questions of central importance. It is
necessary to clarify the relationship between increases in expenditures that
will result from implementing the project and those resulting from :Ithe govlern-
ment's other policies and investments to improve quality and equity, notably
the TS and the associated modifications in the SL. Annex 5, Table 1 shows
actual and projected recurrent spending for education, including the effect of
the TS and the SL, plus recurrent costs that will result from the project for
the period 1990 through 2004. From the viewpoint of sustainability, it is
important to bear in mind not only the recurrent cost implications of the
project but also other Government policies that will further entail recurrent
costs. Government has made a political commitment to the TS and changes in
the SL and has already provided funds in the 1991 budget for their implementa-
tion. Annex 5, Table 2 shows estimates of these costs, which will increase to
38.7 billion pesos in 1995. This amounts to 11.6 percent of the estiuimted
sector budget in that year.



- 40 -

4.09        The total projected increase in recurrent spending in education
during the years 1991 through 1997 will be approximately 60 percent (Annex 5,
Table 1) or 8.2 percent per year, including increases that would take place
due to system growth, the TS and the SL, and the effects of implementing the
project. This substantial increase in sector spending will be necessary to
sustain quality improvements and targeted programs for schools with difficul-
ties, and to maintain decentralization on a sound and realistic financial
basis. While this is a rapid increase, it is not unreasonable for a sector to
which government has given a high priority. It implies that education's share
of public spending (which is projected to grow at 4.5 percent per year) would
increase from 9 percent of estimated total public expenditures in 1991 to a
high of 11.6 percent in 1995, after which it would diminish slightly to less
than 11 percent. This share of public spending would be below the average for
lower middle income countries, comparable to Chile, and would not be high in
comparison with the 1988 average for all of Latin America (11 percent). The
Government is fully aware of the need to limit budgetary expansion in the
education sector and that the sector budget must remain within the parameters
established at the macro level. It has considered the projected increases
with care and is confident that they are sustainable. In view of the impor-
tance of the budget issue, it was agreed during negotiations that the Govern-
ment and the Bank will review the education budget annually with the objective
of insuring that: (a) adequate resources are provided to sustain the quality
improvement program and for effective decentralization; and (b) proposed
sector spending levels are consistent with the macroeconomic framework.
F. Project Economic Benefits
4.10        Although the principal benefits that will result from the proposed
project are improvements in the quality of basic education in Chile, it is
possible to calculate certain benefits that can be measured in money terms.
There are two categories of benefits that can be readily and appropriately
estimated: (a) cost savings resulting from reductions in the numbers of
children repeating grades and, thus, in the average number of years to
complete the eight grades of primary school; and (b) economic benefits that
will accrue to students completing basic education who otherwise would have
dropped out of school. With regard to cost savings, the project's quality-
improvement components will result in greater efficiency in use of costly
inputs because students who are learning effectively will have less tendency
to repeat grades or drop out of school. Present repetition rates are over
12.0 percent when adjusted for dropouts who subsequently reenroll in other



- 41 -
schools (see Annex 6, Tables 2 and 3). It requires 12.4 student-years on the
average for each child who completes the eight-year basic education cycle
(Annex 6, Table 5). This is a high rate of inefficiency for an education
system as well-established as Chile's; but the national average figures cited
do not fully reflect the even worse situation that exists in many schools
serving rural and low-income communities (Annex 6, Tables 2 and 3). As these
data indicate that over 200,000 student places are occupied by repeaters,
there is ample margin for improvement in internal efficiency. Given that
Chile's net primary education enrollment ratio is already very close to
100 percent, the number of children entering grade one will remain approxi-
mately the same. Total enrollment, once the project's quality-enhancement
components begin to have their effect, will thus be lower than it would have
been if repetition rates remained the same. The value of these cost savings
(adjusted to real value in 1996) would be 46.8 billion pesos, or approximately
US$135.7 million (Annex 5, Table 3).
4.11        The second category of benefits arises because fewer students will
fall behind and drop out and greater numbers of students will complete all
eight years of basic education. The ec-enomic benefits of primary education
have been well established. Considering only the economic benefits accruing
to the increased number of students completing eight years of basic education,
these are estimated to be 21.7 billion pesos (again adjusted to value as of
1996), or approximately US$62.9 million (Annex 5, Table 3).
4.12        These two categories of benefits that can be quantified and
expressed in money terms--savings in public costs of education and added
earnings of basic education graduates--constitute only a part of the benefits
of the project. These monetary benefits alone amount to a significant
US$200.0 million: more than ths amount of the loan and over 80.0 percent of
total project costs.
V. IMPLEMENTATION. PROCUREMENT, DISBURSEMENT, AUDITING.
MONITORING AND SUPERVISION
A. Status of Preparation
5.01        Project preparation, was financed by the Government and the Bank's
Project Preparation Facility. Each project component and subcomponent
discussed with the Bank is consistent with sector policies, objectives, and
strategies, as indicated in Annex 7. Since the project preparation coordina-
tor reports directly and or an ongoing basis to the Minister and Under-
secretary of Education, the project enjoys substantial support and commitment.
The counterpart funds for 1991 in the amount of US$4.3 million have been
included in the Government's FY91 budget (Iaw No. 19.012 of December 10,
1990).
B. Implementation Schedule
5.02        The project is expected to be implemented over a 6-year period,
from January 1992 to December 1997. Project activities started after apprais-
al (June 1991), at which time, retroactive financing was considered in order
to start the gradual implementation of PRODEP., teacher training, and key
project's studies. The implementation schedule is shown in Annex 10.



- 42 -
C. Prolect ManaAement
5.03        Implementation Structure.  The MINEDUC would be responsible for
overall implementation of the project. To assist in the oversight of project
implementation, the MINEDUC will establish a Project Implementation Unit
(PIU), reporting to the Minister or Under Secretary level. The PIU comprises
a matrix-type managerial organization with four programmatic units and three
service units as shown in the organizational chart of Annex 11. The program-
matic uinits will be in charge of coordinating and monitoring the implementa-
tion of their respective project components, whereas the service units will be
respolisible for a wide range of activities including: (a) the planning,
programming, monitoring and evaluating activities of the project as a whole;
(b) procurement, civil works, technical assistance, and personnel; and
(c) budgeting, financing, disbursement, accounting, and auditing. The heads
of the seven units will report directly to the PIU's manager, who de facto
operates as the MECE's general project coordinator. The PIU would be respon-
sible for coordination with the Bank and other Ministries, agencies, institu-
tions, and NGOs involved in project implementation. The project manager would
be assisted in policy and monitoring activities by a project Executive Council
(EC) comprised of the Minister of Education or his/her representative, the
Under Secretary of Education, the Directors of MINEDUC'S Division of Planning
and Budgeting and the Division of Education, and the Director of the CPEIP.
The PIU will also comprise a small number of experienced senior managers who
will use the Ministry's existing infrastructure to implement operational
activities. The PIU's manager would be assisted by a legal advisor. The EC
would meet regularly to review project implementation, address policy,
strategy, and organizational problems that could affect project execution, and
review project monitoring indicators. The Minister of Education and the Under
secretary would be assisted by an Advisory Council constituted by highly
respected personalities representing the education, cultural, and private
sectors, as well as political groups. The Council would meet twice a year to
provide guidance based on how society perceives the effects of MECE. During
project Jmplementation, the PIU will employ about 12 senior level profession-
als, 3 technical staff, 6 support staff. Annex 11 describes in more detail
the functions and responsibilities of each PIU's unit. The project will be
executed by different entities. Operational agreements will be signed by
MINEDUC with all autonomous entities (paras. 3.07, 3.23, 3.36, and 3.42).
Given the project complexity and the large number of entities involved in
project implementation, at appraisal the Bank discussed specific arrangement
with local consultants and specialist from UNESCO and UNICEF regional offices
to supervise the project. Agreement was reached during negotiations that the
Government will employ a project manager with qualifications and under terms
of employment satisfactory to the Bank. MINEDUC's decree establishing the PIU
would be a condition of loan effectiveness.
D. Procurement
5.04        Table 5.1 summarizes procurement under the Bank Loan.
5.05        Civil Works.  The bulk of civil works comprises adaptation of
rural facilities donated by the communities into small rural preschool
centers, refurbishing of existing deprived primary schools, and construction
of small urban preschool and primary classroom extensions. Procurement of
these works would follow Bank guideliaes and standard bidding documents would
be used. The draft of these bidding documents were reviewed during appraisal
and submitted to LACVP procurement advisor for final approval.

c/ Includes: training, US$9.5 million; health services, US$6.9 million;
local salaries (including monetary incentives for teachers upgrading and

million.
(a) Small preschool and primary classroom extensions works are expected
to cost, at most, US$10,000 each, and corresponding contracts would be
awarded on the basis of price quotation obtained from at least three
eligible contractors up to an aggregate amount of US$4.8 million. In
the few cases where various civil works could be grouped together in
packages costing more than US$50,000, these would be awarded on the
basis of local competitive bidding (LCB) procedures satisfactory to the
Bank. Such packages are not expected to exceed US$100,000 and US$3.2
million in total.
(b) The rehabilitation of classrooms to be executed by the Municipali-
ties, as well as the rural preschool adaptation to be executed by
INTEGRA, are expected to cost no more than US$8,000 per school. It
includes: school furniture repairs; painting; lighting repairs; kitchen
and toilet installations (for the rural preschool centers only); water
and sewage installations; and replacement of windows, doors, floors, and
roofs. Neither international competitive bidding (ICB) or LCB would be



- 44 -
suitable given the nature of these works. Therefore, adaptation and
rehabilitation contracts not exceeding US$8,000 and US$31 4 million in
aggregate would be awarded either on the basis of three price quotations
up to an aggregate amount of US$18.8 million, or when price quotations
cannot be obtained, through direct contracting up to an aggregate amount
of US$12.6 million.
5.06        Goods and Materials.  The bulk of goods and materials comprises
computer and peripheral equipment, school furniture, teaching materials and
equipment, and textbooks. ICB and LCB procurement will be done in accordance
with Bank guidelines and using standard bidding documents to be agreed with
the Bank. The draft documents have been submitted to the LACVP procurement
advisor for final approval.
(a) Contracts for the procurement of computers and peripheral equip-
ment, school furniture, and materials and equipment, will be awarded
following ICB (including 15 percent preference for local bidders) in
accordance with the Bank's guidelines (May 1985) when valued above
US$250,000 each. For contracts valued between US$20,000 and US$250,000,
procurement would be carried out following LCB procedures satisfactory
to the Bank up to an aggregate amount of US$2.2 million equivalent. For
contracts valued below US$20,000 equivalent, shopping procedures,
satisfactory to the Bank, would be followed up to an aggregate amount of
US$2.0 million equivalent. Computer and peripheral equipment will be
procured following a two-step ICB procurement procedure in agreement
with Bank guidelines.
(b) Textbooks will be awarded on the basis of two-step ICB procedures,
in accordance with Bank guidelines for all contracts valued above
US$250,000 each. Contracts valued below US$250,000 would be awarded on
the basis of LCB procedures in accordance with procurement procedures
acceptable to the Bank, and up to a maximum aggregate limit of
US$21.3 million equivalent.
(c) Educational materials and manuals will be awarded on the basis of
ICB procedures in accordance with Bank's guidelines for all contracts
valued above US$250,000 each. Contracts valued below US$250,000 would
be awarded on the basis of LCB in accordance with Government procurement
procedures acceptable to the Bank, and up to a maximum aggregate limit
of US$2.1 million equivalent.
(d) School feeding contracts totalling US$15.3 million are not expected
to exceed the equivalent of US$250,000 per contract and would be awarded
through LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank. Contracts valued at
below US$20,000 would be awarded on the basis of price quotations
obtained from three eligible suppliers up to an aggregate amount of
US$5.5 million equivalent. When price quotations cannot be obtained,
direct contracting procedures would be used for purchases not exceeding
US$5,000 per school, and US$1.2 million equivalent in aggregate.
5.07        PRODEPE.  The maximum cost of any project to be financed through
PRODEPE would not exceed US$8,000, the average amount being about US$6,000.
Such projects may include goods (paper, pencils, ink, materials) and services
(transportation, consultants fees). Procedures to rank and select projects
are stated in paras. 3.04 through 3.08 and Annex 8. Given its nature, these
contracts with a total value of US$38.4 million will be awarded on the basis



- 45 -
of price quotations obtained from at least three eligible suppliers, and when
price quotations cannot be obtained through direct contracting up to an
aggregate amount of US$15.5 million equivalent.
5.08        Technical Assistance.  Consultants carrying out studies and
technical assistance will be hired following Bank guidelines (August 1981).
Contracts for small consulting assignments valued at US$20,000 or less, would
be formalized on the basis of standard terms of reference and employment
conditions and would not require prior review by the Bank.
5.09        Items not Involving Procurement.  The project includes the
financing of inservice training, health services, local salaries (including
monetary incentives for teachers upgrading and lecturing in deprived urban and
rural primary schools), evaluation and supervision and operational expendi-
tures for up to an aggregate amount of US$90.2 million.
5.10        Prior Review.  Prior review by the Bank would be required for all
procurement documentation for ICB contracts and for the first three LCB
contracts for goods and civil works, respectively, valued above US$50,000
every year of the project. Furthermore, all documentation for the hiring of
consultants for contracts valued above US$20,000 would also be subject to
prior review by the Bank. All other contracts would be reviewed in the field
during Bank supervision missions. The Bank would also sample on a periodic
basis the procurement practices followed in the implementation of the quality
improvement projects financed by the PRODEPE.
E. Account and Disbursements
5.11        The proceeds of the proposed loan are expected to be disbursed in
accordance with the schedule shown in Annex 12, over a period of six and a
half years, which is longer than the Bank's average country profile for Chile
(5.5 years), but shorter than the profile for Latin America (7 years). The
disbursement period was determined after taking into account the advanced
status of the project designs, MINEDUC's execution capacity, and the yearly
budget availability approved by the Ministry of Finance. Annex 12 also
illustrates in greater detail the flow of funds that will be channelled to the
different entities included in the project. The allocation of loan amount avd
the disbursement percentages are summarized in Table 5.2. Disbursement would
be made against certified Statements of Expenditures, except for contracts,
valued at above US$200,000 and contracts requiring prior review.
5.12        Based on the experience of the "900 Deprived Primary Schools
Program" and the "Programa Nacional de Fomento al Desarrollo de Innovaciones
Educativas" (para. 3.08), the MINEDUC will set up, in conjunction with the
Chilean financial authorities, suitable mechanisms to facilitate disbursement
of: (a) resources allocated to quality improvement projects in the framework
of PRODEPE; (b) resources intended for preschool and primary classroom
construction; (c) resources assigned for rural preschool and primary school
refurbishing; and (d) resources to procure goods and services during project
implementation. Confirmation of adoption of agreements with the Municipali-
ties and legal entities different from MINEDUC for the transfer of resources,
with terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank to implement such
agreements, will be a condition for loan effectiveness.



- 46 -
5.13        To reduce the interval between payments made by the beneficiaries
and receipt of reimbursements from the Bank, a Special Account would be estab-
lished in the Central Bank of Chile. The Special Account would be denominated
in US dollars and would be established with an initial deposit of US$8.0
million, the average four-month requirement. Project expenditures would be
monitored by the PIU. The PIU would request replenishment of the Special
Account on the basis of standard disbursement procedures.

5.14        The Bank would finance up to US$6 millions (approximately 4
percent of the proposed loan) retroactively for expenditures incurred after
June 15, 1991 and in accordance with Bank procurement guidelines. Retroactive
financing is required so that the Government may begin the gradual implementa-
tion of the pedagogical decentralization program, textooks, teacher training,
and key project's studies as soon as possible, and to facilitate the timely
start up of the project.
F. Audits
5.15        The Bank would require that an independent auditor acceptable to
the Bank: (a) audit all accounts, SOEs, and Special Accounts; (b) apply
auditing standards and procedures satisfactory to the Bank and conforming to
generally accepted auditing practices; (c) carry out its auditing work in a
timely manner (an annual report would be presented no later than six months
after the end of each calendar year); and (d) render an audit opinion or the
reasons whereby such opinion cannot be rendered. Given the geographical
dispersion of PRODEPE, the Bank required that basic elements of conventional
financial management, accounting and auditing should be included specifically
for PRODEPE in the auditing procedures of the project.
G. Proiect Monitorinz, Evaluation and Sugervision
5.16        In line with the project objectives, key efficiency, quality, and
equity indicators (Annex 13, Table 1) will be monitored to track and support
improvements in the overall efficiency and effectiveness of preschool and
primary education. In order to support spot-checking along the project's life
in any of its subcomponents, a detailed project subcomponent matrix is
provided in Annex 13, Tables 2 through 6.
5.17        Overall monitoring and evaluation of the project would be the
responsibility of the PIU. Specific tasks include: (a) preparing annual
progress reports on project implementation, which would be submitted to the



- 48 -
Bank by December 30 of each year; (b) measuring quantitative and qualitative
objectives achieved against the monitoring indicators shown in the implementa-
tion matrix; (c) updating and reviewing annually project implementation
schedules and action plans; and (d) preparing a Project Completion Report that
would be submitted to the Bank no later than six months after the closing date
of the Loan. Beside regular Bank scheduled supervision missions, UNESCO and
U'NICEF specialists will supervise the project under specific arrangements
dixocussed durir.g the appraisal (para. 5.03). The project also supports
Vreinarclh oriented to evaluate the impact of this project, as well as other NGO
prvjects targeted to enhance the quality of primary education and to improve
the equity of the educational system (para. 3.50). Annex 13, Table 7
delineates the supervision plan, including the skill mix needed, frequency of
mnissions, project launch seminar, annual early-term and mid-term reviews,
regular Bank and specific supervision schedules to be provided by UNESCO's and
UNICEF's regional offices, as well as local and international consultants.
Supervision arrangements are expected to result in a requirement of about 17
staffweeks (SWs) per year.
5.18        Annual early-term and mid-term project reviews will be focussing
on: (i) TS and the result of the application of the new subsidy formula
implementation; (ii) PRODEPE's implementation process; (iii) preschool
expansion and quality improvements; (iv) implementation of the EIS;
(v) fulfillment of the loan covenants; (vi) impact of the implementation of
each component on the efficiency, equity, and quality of preschool and primary
education; (vii) impact on strengthening the decentralization process; and
(viii) the PIU's performance. Agreement was reached during negotiations that
such reviews will take place. The content of the annual early-term and mid-
term project reviews was agreed during negotiations.
VI. EXPECTED BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Expected Benefits
6G01        The principal benefits of the proposed project will be in terms of
improvements in the efficiency and quality of education, mainly for the
children in the 5,000 schools most directly affected by the project, but also
in terms of residual benefits of better quality for all of Chile's basic
education facilities. Through better teaching and other inputs into the
education process, students will attain higher levels of cognitive achievement
and problem-solving abilities necessary to perform successfully in a rapidly-
charlging modern economy. These benefits of educational quality are difficult
to quarntify. There are, however, certain benefits for which we can calculate
aconomic values. As discussed in Chapter IV and Annex 5, these will include
coEt savings resulting from 1.wer repetition and dropouts and, therefore,
fewer years needed to complete the eight grades primary school (savings are
estiraated to be 46.8 billion pesos or US$135.7 million), and economic benefits
that will accrue to the roughly 10,000 additional students who will complete
basic education each year instead of dropping out. The incremental economic
benefits are estimated at 21.7 billion pesos (US$62.9 million). In both cases
these estimates of benefits have been discounted to 1996. Bearing in mind
th-at these are the only benefits that can be readily calculated in monetary
torii-. their total value of about US$200.0 million is substantial.



- 49 -
6.02        The targeting criteria of preschool expainsion and PRODEPE will
ensure a poverty alleviation impact by favoring in the first case low-ineormo,
poor chi:Ldren, and in the second, high-risk, mostly poor, urban and rural
subsidized primary schools.
6.03        The participation of teachers and school principals in the design
of quality improvement projects in the framework of the pedagogical decentra-
lization, of supervisors in their ranking, and the municipal and provIncial
authorities, as well as the provincial education Councils in their seleetion,
will strengthen the decentralization process.
6.04        One of the project's components will specifically provide informa-
tion and stimulate policy debate on the objectives, benefits, and costs of the
present system of secondary education and will consider alternative strategies
for modernizing this subsector and improving its quality in cost-effective
ways. From this body of studies, conferences with national and international
experts and analysis of policy alternatives there may emerge a proposal for a
project to improve secondary education.
6.05        Finally, the proposed project will include institutional streng-
thening measures that will allow: (a) MINEDUC to translate educational
policies and strategies into concrete programmatic actions, targeting
resources to deprived schools, and periodically monitoring sector achievements
and problems through improved sector programming, budgeting, information and
analysis capabilities at its central, regional, and provincial levels of
decision-making; (b) the municipalities to execute more effectively education-
al programs derived from sector policies education system, and administer more
efficiently the resources of their assigned municipal schools; and (c) school
principals and proprietors to implement adequately technical-pedagogical
supervision and school management. One short-term benefit expected from the
EIS implementation will be the timely detection of possible wastage in
resource allocation and a better deployment of teachers and other educational
resources.
B. Risks
6.06        The major risks to project implementation are:  (a) possible
distortions in targeting project interventions; and (b) recentralization of
education services. These risks will be reduced by: (a) implementing precise
targeting criteria to benefit deprived urban and rural schools; (b) adopting
suitable operational and procedural manuals and training for provincial and
municipal education authorities, school principals, and supervisors; (c)
carefully selecting and training key personnel participating in the Project
Implementation Unit, and in PRODEPE's disbursement and monitoring; (d)
promoting the participation of the municipalities and communities in project
implementation; and (e) close supervision by the Bank.
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.01        Prior to negotiations, the Government submitted the following
documents for Bank's review and agreement:
(a)   draft of the policy statement for the education sector specifying:
(i) medium-term goals regarding the equity, efficiency and qiltlity



- 50 -
of primary education; (ii) the overall sector financing plan for
primary, secondary and tertiary levels of the education system;
(iii) the responsibilities at various levels of Government and the
private sector; and (iv) that the Government will allocate
requisite financial resources to sustain future education sector
expenditures (para. 2.54); This policy statement has now been
finalised.
(b)   PRODEPE's Operational Manual officially approved by MINEDUC
(para. 3.05);
(c)   the final version of the Pedagogical Supervision Manual
(para. 3.10);
(d)   the final version of the Administrative Inspection Manual
(para. 3.45); and
(e)   the basic elements of conventional financial management accounting
and auditing to be included in the implementation of PRODEPE
(para. 5.15).
7.02        At negotiations, agreements were reached that:
(a)   changes to the:  PRODEPE Manual (para. 3.05), Supervision Manual
(para 3.10); TS Law (Law 19070 dated July 1, 1991) (para. 3.27),
and Inspector Manual (para. 3.45) that materially and adversely
affect the project would be considered an event of default;
(b)   the Government will put into effect a system no later than June
30, 1992 whereby the entire teaching force will be encouraged to
participate in periodic upgrading and inservice teaching training
during the life of the project (para. 3.28);
(c)   annual budgets allocate each year of project implementation
specific minimum amount required to increase subsidy to small,
mostly rural schools (para. 3.29);
(d)   the Government will furnish to the Bank for review the education
budget annually to ensure that adequate resources are provided to
sustain the quality improvement program and the strengthening of
the decentralization process, and that the proposed sector
spending levels are consistent with the macroeconomic framework
(para. 4.09);
(e)   the Government will employ a project manager with qualification
and under terms of employment satisfactory to the Bank (para.
5.03; and
(f)   an annual early-term and mid-term project reviews will be carried
out (para. 5.18).
7.03        For Loan Effectiveness, the Bank would require that:
(a)   the project manuals (PRODEPE, supervision and administrative
inspection) be adopted by MINEDUC (paras. 3.05, 3.10 and 3.45);



- 51 -
(b)   model contract(s) be signed either with the Municipalities or
private-subsidized school proprietors concerning PRODEPE resource
allocation to the municipalities, INTEGRA, and JUNJI relating to
didactic materials, additional personnel, school feeding program,
classroom construction, and preschool and primary school refur-
bishing and remodelling (paras. 3.07, 3.23, 3.36, and 3.42), with
terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank;
(c)   Government Bylaws regulating inservice teacher training certif ica-
tion based on selectivity and quality control merits be enacted
(para. 3.28);
(d)   the specific amount be allocated by the Government in the 1992
bu6get to increase the subsidy to small, mostly rural schools
(para. 3.29); and
(e)   the PIU be formally established (para. 5.03).
7.04        To commence disbursements of the primary school textbook and
preschool teaching materials components, the Bank will require the adoption of
the cost-recovery policy for primary school textbooks and preschool teaching
materials (paras. 3.17 and 3.39).
Recommendations
7.05        Subject to the above assurances and conditions, the proposed
project would constitute a suitable basis for a Bank loan of US$170 million
for the Republic of Chile, repayable in 17 years including 4 years of grace,
at the Bank's standard variable interest rate.

Annex 2

Annex 2: DESCRIPTION AND ORGANIZATIONAL CHARTS OF THE MINISTRY OF EDUCATION,
ITS REGIONAL AND PROVINCIAL OFFICES, AND THE MUNICIPAL EDUCATION DEPARTMENT
1.     MINEDUC carries its legally mandated functions of policy setting,
supervision, evaluation, and finAncial monitoring through its central,
regional, and provincial departments, largely independent of the municipali-
ties, which are in charge of the daily operation of the public schools. To
perform these activities, MINEDUC is divided into one Subsecretariat with four
divisions (Primary and Secondary Education, Higher Education, Culture, and
Planning and Budgeting), two departments (Legal and Administrative Services),
and one center (Teacher Training and Educational Research-CPEIP). The MINEDUC
has 13 Regional offices (SEREMIS) located in each of the 13 geopciitical and
administrative regions of the country, which in turn are divided into 40
educational Provincial departments. In addition, there are three decentral-
ized institutions in the eeucation sector: Junta Nacional de Jardines de
Infantiles (JUNJI), providing preschool services; Junta Nacional de Auxilio
Escolar y Becas (JUNAEB), in charge of the school feeding and fellowship
programs; and the Comision Nacional de Investigaci6n Cientifica y Tecnologica
(CONICYT), fostering and guiding scientific and technological research under-
taken in different research centers across the country (chart 1).
2.     The Undersecretary of Education is in charge of the following func-
tions: (a) planning and programming activities and allocating resources to
municipalities and proprietary schools; (b) paying municipalities and private-
subsidized schools according to student-days of actual class attendance;
(c) checking the performance and supervising the decentralized operation of
schools; (d) improving the quality of the educational services delivered by
the system; (e) sponsoring cultural affairs; and (f) coordinating the MINEDUC
with universities and several public autonomous institutions.
3.     The 280 Municipal Education Departments (DEM) have the responsibility
to administer the Municipal schools including personnel and financial
administration. The 54 Municipal Corporations are private organizations lead
by a board of directors (regulated by the Bylaws of private organizations)
that are entrusted to manage private subsidized schools (chart 2).
4.     Preschool services are provided through three principal channels.  The
first comprises municipal, private subsidized and private-paid preschools,
whose coverage is targetted exclusively to 5 and 6 year olds. The second
major group includes publicly financed programs run by an autonomous agency of
the MINEDUC, the National Board of Kinderga:tens (Junta Nacional de Jardines
de Infantiles--JUNJI), under two modalitiea: JUNJI and Jardines Infantiles
Familiares, formerly CADEL. Both of these programs provide integrated health,
nutrition, and education services, and promote social and psychomotor develop-
ment through stimulation-oriented activities targetted to preschoolers 2- to
5-years of age who are from poor families. The third principal vehicle are
preschool programs run by a Government-assisted agency INTEGRA (formerly
FUNACO), and private, nonprofit entities and NGOs, including CARITAS.



- 55 -
Annex 2
Page 2 of 4
5.     Preschool education employs 9,138 preschool teachers and almost 7,000
paraprofessionals (Table 9, Annex 3). Out of a total of 16,000 people
employed, 57 percent are qualified, trained teachers and 43 percent are
paraprofessionals working in the nonconventional models (JUNJI and INTEGRA),
and in need of further inservice training. The student teacher ratio of the
municipal (29) and private subsidized preschool (35) alternatives, conform to
the average reported by UNESCO/OREALC for Latin America and the Caribbean
(LAC) as a region; these ratios are much higher than in the corresponding
private-paid preschools (20). The nonconventional modalities of JUNJI and
INTEGRA rely extensively on paraprofessionals, with relatively low number of
preschool teachers working mostly as supervisors.
6.     Over 105,000 children, mostly poor, were offered during 1989 integrated
health, nutrition, and early stimulation in preschool programs. JUNAEB,
created in 1960, administers the school feeding and scholarship programs.
JUNAEB procures the distribution, preparation, and serving of meals to
children through eligible prospective bidders from the private sector. This
program is oriented to children under 6 years of age and provides up to 800
daily calories and 15 to 20 grams of protein.
7.     The total primary education school population is being taught by more
than 67,400 teachers, 70 percent of whom work in municipal schools,
23.0 percent in private-subsidized institutions, and 7 percent in paid-private
schools. Average pupils per teacher are: 26.1 in the municipal schools, 39.6
in the private subsidized schools, and 28.4 in the private paid. The national
average is 29.4. There are indications of overstaffing in the municipal
primary schools of Temuco (Region IX), Puerto Montt (Region X) and Cohyaique
(Region XI) where the average pupils-teacher ratio is under 25, below the
Chilean national average and slightly smaller than the average ratios reported
by UNESCO/OREALC for Latin America and the Caribbean as a region (27).
However, only the thorough analysiLt of the Education Information System (EIS)
at the municipal level (para. 3.49) can determine if the over- staffing
hypothesis is valid.
8.     During 1989, the MINEDUC distributed over 1.9 million new books free
and redistributed about 5.2 million more, reaching over 2.0 million students.
The books from first to third grade are renewed annually as they are used for
student exercises; the books from fourth to eighth grades remain at the school
for two or three school cycles. Texts are accompanied by teacher guides that
describe learning objectives, provide suggestions, and give answers to the
book's exercises. Until 1983, textbooks were only provided to primary school
students; since then, some secondary courses have also become eligible.
According to a recent survey, over 50 percent of the textbooks distributed
were received by the poorest 30 percent of the population. The unit cost of
newly acquired books in 1989 averaged about US$0.80. The Government has
agreed to implement a cost-recovery policy targetted on primary textbooks
(para. 3.17).
