Annex 4: EDUCATION SECTOR BUDGET AND FINANCING
1.     Table I of this Annex shows the executed education budget for the 1980-
1990 period in relation to gross product and to the total federal budget.
While there have been fluctuations in both these relationships, the general
trend in education's share has been downward. With 2.5 percent of gross
product and 8.1 percent of public spending in 1990, Chile had one of the
lowest levels of public educaticnal spending in the region. Other important
changes in educational spending, have been: (a) the initiation and rapid
increase in transfers of resources from the central government to private and
municipal schools via per capita subsidies or subventions; and (b) the
reduction in the share of post-secondary education in total sector spending.
2.     Table 2 of this Annex shows the rapid growth of subventions as a share
of public spending on education. The table presents both the budget as
originally estimated and enacted into law and the actual, executed budget,
which has tended to be somewhat higher due to supplementary payments toward
the end of each year. Table 3 of this Annex shows the relative shares of each
subsector in total educational spending in constant pesos of 1990 for the
years since 1980. The decline in the share of post-secondary education, which
received 38 percent of public spending in the sector in 1980 but only 19
percent in 1990, derives from the reforms introduced by the Government during
1981 to decrease central financing in higher education and promote private
participation. The most important beneficiaries of this shift were primary
education and the preschool level, where the percentage increase was large but
the absolute amount relatively small.
3.     In Chile, the costs per student per year unit costs in constant pesos
do not differ greatly between levels of education. There is little difference
between the costs of preschool, primary or basic education, and the secondary
level, while even public spending per university student is only three to four
times the amount spent on primary school students. Annex 4, Table 4 shows
Preschool costs are actually higher than the costs for primary education, and
costs of general secondary education (called the Scientific-Humanistic stream)
are not much greater than primary education costs.
4.     As is typically the case, the largest share of the education budget
goes for teachers' salaries. In Chile's education budget, this is not
immediately evident because some 60 percent of the total budget goes for
subventions. But since salaries account for on the order of 90 percent of the
subventions at the primary education level and substantial portions at other
levels, it is clear that Chile is no exception to the usual picture. Amounts
spent for school building and maintenance has been below 1.0 percent of the
total education budget. Since the decentralization to the municipalities,
most capital spending for education falls under the Ministry of Interior
budget. Even taking both Ministries' capital spending into account, this item
has represented a small share of sector spending, even in its highest years,
because of economic austerity. Table 5 of this Annex shows combined capital
spending by purpose.

5.     Discussion of the public budget for education would be incomplete
without taking into account two factors: the contributions of the municipali-
ties to spending for primary and secondary schools, and the role of the
private sector in financing education. In both cases there have been
significant contributions to total education spending but, as discussed in the
following sections, the main source of educational finance continues to be the
public education budget.
The Role of the Municipalities in Financing Primary and Secondary Education
6.     Decentralization of education gave municipal governments (and in some
cases, municipal corporations) and private-subsidized schools responsibility
for providing educational services. Despite this shift, the Central
Government has continued to be the most important provider of educational
finance because the decentralized authorities operate on the basis of per
capita subventions. But because the subventions have not kept pace with costs
of the municipal education programs, and because of shif*s in enrollment from
the public schools to the private sector and concommitan;. declines in subven-
tion payments, municipalities have incurred deficits in their education
budgets and have had to add payments from other sources in order to cover
these daficits.



- 71 -
Annex 4
Page 9 of 13
7.     Municipal schools provide places for approximately 59 percent of public
school students at both the primary and secondary education levels. Total
payments of subventions for education to the municipalities amounted to 87.7
billion pesos in 1990, or about 40 percent of the total budget of the central
Ministry of Education. By far the largest source of funding for municipal
schools is transfers via the subventions, either directly to the
municipalities or to public corporations that manage their schools.'/
According to the 1987 CASEN II household survey, subeidies for secondary
education are concentrated in higher income groups, opposite to the case with
preschool and primary education subsidy transfers. The Ministry of Hacienda
pays subventions to the municipality which, in turn, transfers the funds to
its education department or corporation. Like the private-subsidized schools,
those operated by the municipal education departments or corporations are
subject to periodic verification or audit of attendance on which the subven-
tion payments are based. The municipal education departments are obliged to
render accounts indicating the uses of the funds.
8.     In the case of many municipalities, even the substantial amount of
subvention payments does not cover the full costs of the municipal schools,
and these municipalities have had to pay additional sums from resources other
than the subventions that are intended to cover education costs. In munici-
palities that do not receive sufficient subvention payments to cover their
education costs, there are several alternative ways of making up the short-
fall. Other sources of funds include the following:
(a) Use by the municipalities of funds allocated to them from the
Department of Interior's Fondo Comfin Municipal (FCM). The FCM is
derived from 60 percent of land taxes and 50 percent of vehicle
registration fees collected throughout the county, as well as special
amounts from the municipalities of Santiago, Las Condes and Providencia,
and is distributed according to a progressive formula that gives poor
communities larger shares.
(b) Funds distributed by the Ministry of Interior and intended to meet
special needs of communities, called the Fondo Nacional de Desarrollo
Regional (FNDR). The total use of funds from the FCM and FNDR to cover
educational costs in excess of the subventions was 7.9 billion pesos in
1990.
(c) Extraordinary payments by Hacienda to cover deficits in municipal
finances, including those in education departments and corporations.
These vary from year to year, and are not paid in all years. The amount
was over 10 billion pesos in 1988, a special large payment to cover
accumulated deficits, and was 2.6 billion pesos in 1990.
1/ Of the total of 327 municipalities, 54 have established corporations.
These include some of the most wealthy (e.g., Las Condes) as well as some
of the poorest. Corporations have somewhat greater flexibility in managing
the schools, but are subject to the same verification of attendance and
other controls as municipal schools.



- 72 -
Annex 4
Page 10 of 13
(d) Possible use of funds generated by the municipalities from totally
local sources that do not form part of the FCM, but these amounts would
be extremely small for most municipalities.
9.    The Contraloria General !e la Republica reported that the combined
amounts used by the provinces from their FCM and FNDR resources, plus the
extraordinary payment of 2.6 billion pesos, was 10.5 billion pesos in 1990, or
approximately 11 percent of total spending on the municipal schools.  In 1990,
municipalities' coitribution to total education finance (all sources) amounted
to 3.2 percent.  There is unquestionably a problem of excess of costs of
municipal schools over revenues, and this problem may be severe in the case of
some municipalities. The total magnitude of payments in addition to the
subventions is not overwhelmingly large, and will be somewhat reduced as a
result of the law modifying the subventions. The Bank has asked for a study
to be performed whicn will examine the relation between subvention payments
and use of own resources in a sample of municipalities.
10.   An obvious question is: why is there a shortfall?  A discussion of the
adequacy of the subvention payments since decentralization took place and of
the managerial efficiency of the municipal education systems and related
matters transcends the scope of this annex. Government recognizes the problem
and proposes to deal with it through a proposed reform of the subvention law,
which is linked to the TS now before the legislature. This modification would
add an estimated total of 5 billion pesos over a 5-year perio' to the basic
subventions, and would then be phased out. One of the objectives of the TS
and the modification of the subventions is to adjust the payments to
municipalities and private-subsidized schools more closely to the real costs
of providing the education. While there will be an increase in the Ministry
of Education budget due to increases in subventions and the other effects of
the TS, this increase in transfers to the municipalities will be at least
partly offset by a decrease in transfers such as Hacienda's extraordinary
payments. The education budget will reflect more realistically the full cost
of education, municipalities will receive more adequate flows of funds for
education, and there may be an incentive for municipalities to manage their
education systems efficiently in order to operate within the amounts intended
for education.
The Contributions of the Private Sector
11.   Private provision of educational services has increased rapidly
following the decentralization of education in Chile, mainly in the private
subeidized schools. Enrollment in privately-subsidized schools at the primary
education level has increased from 306,200 or roughly 15 percent of total
primary education enrollment in 1980 to 623,100 or approximately one third of
enrollment at this level. Note that these schools receive essentially all
their financing through subventions and, while they represent an important
change in the direction of private sector operation of schools and of diversi-
ty of options for parents and students, this does not constitute a major
increase in private sector financing of education. Municipalities provide
additional funding from their own resources to cover shortfalls in their
education budgets, but private operators cannot do this and survive. Private-



- 73 -
Annex 4
Page 11 of 13
subsidized schools at the primary education level are not supposed to charge
fees, but in a number of cases they do so, either on a voluntary basis (which
is not prohibited) or through small specific charges for materials or other
items. There are no data available on family payments for primary education
but annecdotal information indicates that this may add as much as 20 percent
of the subvention or more, especially for private subsidized schools serving
more affluent communities.
12.   The role of fee-paying private education in Chile has not changed
greatly during the period of decentralization, There has always been a
stratum of elite and relatively costly private schools, almost all in urban
areas, and the share of enrollment in private fee-paying institutions has
hardly changed since 1980. Primary education enrollment in private fee-paying
schools in 1989 was 131,100 or 6.5 percent of total primary education enroll-
ment, and 60,140 or 8.1 percent of secondary enrollment. The role of the
private sector was greatest in preschool, where 14.7 percent of 1989 enroll-
ment in the formal (conventional) preschool mode was in paid private schools
(Annex 3, Table 7). Private sector financing of university education is
important both as a percent of total spending for this significant subsector
and as an absolute amount. It is at the post-secondary level that private
sector financing has increased most rapidly in the last decade, as Tables 6
and 7 of this Annex show.

Sources and Relative Shares of Education Sector Financial Resources
13.    How great has the contribution of the private sector and the
municipalities been in total and how has this changed over the decade of the
1980s since decentralization. Since municipal finance of education essential-
ly did not exist prior to the decentralization and the role of private support
of education was limited, the growth in these contributions has been enormous.
In terms of share in total educational spending, there has been a notable
increase, although the public sector continues to provide the greatest part of
total expenditure. The Bank has made estimates of the private sector role.
Annex 4, Table 6 shows total sector spending from Central Government, munici-
pal resources and the private sector from 1985 to 1990. Table 7 presents the
data as percent of total education finance. The main points that emerge from
these two tables are:  (i) non-central government finance (hereafter called
"private", although non-central funding from municipal sources is actually
public and a substantial portion of this is from the Central Government)
represents a significant share of total education finance, increasing from
21.0 to 31.6 percent of the total during the years for which estimates are
available; (ii) the most important contributor to this share--in both absolute
terms and as a source of growth--has been private finance of university
education, which grew from 14.7 to 24.2 percent of total education finance
between 1985 and 1990; (iii) the shares of both the municipal and nonunivers-
ity private contributions increased in percentage terms during the period but
together represent only 7.5 percent of total educational finance; and (iv)
private funds brought total sector finance up to 8.6 percent of GNP in 1985, a
figure well above the regional average, this share has fallen sharply in
recent years. In other words, as important as the role of private finance is,
it has not compensated for the overall decline in resources flowing into the
sector.

Annex 5: RECURRENT EDUCATIONAL EXPENDITURE, PROJECT SUSTAINABILITY
AND THE RELATIONSHIP BETWEEN PROJECT COSTS AND ECONOMIC BENEFITS
1.    As discussed in Chapter IV, the incremental recurrent costs that result
from the project will be on the order of US$22.1 million in 1996, once the
project is fully operational. This Annex considers the financial feasibility
of this increase in recurrent expenditure together with other increases that
are expected to take place as a result of implementing the TS and changing the
subsidy law governing payments to municipal and private schools. It examines
the likely trends in recurrent costs; describes briefly the cost implications
of the TS and the reform of the subsidy law; and explores possible cost
savings and economic benefits that can be expected to result from the project,
in addition to its main effects of enhancing student learning and improving
equity.
Proiection of Recurrent Educational Expenditure
2.    Table l of this Annex presents estimates of recurrent spending in the
education sector to the year 2004, including the estimated recurrent cost
implications of implementing the TS and the revision of the subvention law, as
well as the recurrent costs resulting from the project--both during the life
of the project and afterward.  The basic projection, including the TS, would
result in fairly rapid and sustained growth in sector spending, even in the
absence of the Bank project, taking into account the Government's commitment
to improving tha quality of education and increasing the flow of resources
into the sector. Even starting from this fairly high basis, Annex 5, Table 1
shows that, while education's estimated share of total public spending would
continue to grow rapidly until 1995 due to both the TS and the project, the
share of total public spending is relatively stable and even declines slightly
thereafter. Even at its highest projected level of 11.6 percent of public
spending in 1995, it would be below the average level of public educational
spending in lower middle income countries comparable to Chile (13.3 percent).
It would be slightly higher than the 1988 average for all of Latin America,
which itself had fallen from over 14 percent in 1972.
Impact of the TS on Recurrent Edv.cational Expenditures
3.    The Government has committed itself to providing legislation that will
clarify the professional status and working conditions of teachers, a measure
that apparently enjoys wide political support. This is considered an integral
part of the Government's efforts to improve the quality of education. The
statute recently approved by the Legislature includes provisions about
reachers' pay and allowances and professional issues including inservice
training. Closely related to the statute is a revision of the law governing
subvention payments to municipal and private subsidized schools, since changes
in teachers' pay would have to be financed through these subventions. Table 2
of this Annex presents the estimated costs of implementing the statute.

4.     A portion of the added cost .esulting from the TS will have to do with
on-the-job training for teachers included in the project, and the Government
proposes that this amount be considered as part of its counterpart funding in
support of the project to improve educational quality. The figure in Table 1
of this Annex has been adjusted to avoid double-counting the part of total
project recurrent costs that would result from implementing the TS. In
summary, the analyses indicate that the total increase in the education sector
budget between 1991 and 1997 resulting from the project and the TS and subsidy
changes will be 60 percent or an average annual increase of 8.1 percent, and
that the recurrent costs that will result from the project will amount to some
1.8 percent of the sector budget in 1997.
Estimates of Savings and Economic Benefits Resulting from the Proiect of
USS22.1 Million Resulting from the Proiect
5.     rhe main purpose of this education project is to improve the quality of
basic education in Chile, that is to say, to improve student learning. It
proposes to do this through a program that will be aimed at schools with
lowest academic performance, which includes mostly schools serving poor and
often r-ural communities. This investment in better quality and greater equity
will also lead to cost savings because students receiving better quality
education will not repeat as many grades or drop out of school before
completing the basic education cycle, thus total enrollment is actually
expected to be less as a result of the project than it would have been
otherwise. Moreover, since greater numbers of students will complete all
eight years of basic education, there will be economic benefits in the form of
higher earnings among these additional graduates. These two categories of
benefits--which can be quantified and expressed in money terms as savings in
public costs of education and added income of basic education graduates--do
not constitute all the benefits of the project. It is interesting, however,
to carry out the exercise of estimating its monetizable benefits.



- 79 -
Annex 5
Page 4 of 5
6.     Savings from Reduced Repetition and Dropouts.  Table 3 of this Annex is
based upon two alternative projections of basic education enrollment from 1996
(the year in which the project will be completed and can be expected to have a
favorable impact on repetition and dropouts) to 2005. The first projection
assumes no change in present rates of repetition and the second assumes a
decrease in repetition rates as a result of better teaching, availability of
texts and educational materials and other quality-enhancing inputs. The Table
shows the expected decrease in enrollment as a result of the project. Savings
from reduced enrollment are conservatively estimated on the basis of a
constant cost per student/per year of 63,000 pesos multiplied by the number of
student places saved. Discounting the total savings over the 10-year period
to present value in 1996 (7.5 percent discount rate) yields an estimate of
46.8 billion pesos as of 1996.
7.     Economic Benefits of Increasing Basic EducatIon Graduates.  As is
widely understood, providing primary or basic education is a sound economic
investment, yielding a rate of return that compares favorably with alternative
investments in any sector. The project will result in greater numbers of
basic education graduates because fewer children will drop out of school, and
hence will produce additional economic benefits. One approach to quantifying
these benefits is to calculate the present value of gains in earnings that
result from completing the basic cycle, and multiply this by the number of
additional completers, with appropriate adjustments for the year of comple-
tion. Special analyses of the CASEN II survey performed by the Bank show that
students completing eight years of education earned 36,000 pesos per year
(slightly more that US$100) more than noncompleters in 1987. Table 3 of this
Annex shows the increased numbers of graduates in each year from 1996 to 2005
and the present value (as of 1996, 7.5 percent discount rate) of the added
earnings of these graduates. The total economic benefit thus calculated would
equal 21.7 billion pesos in 1996.

Notes: Each item has three alternatives and godd distractors.
The net scores equals the gross score, less random answers included as
correct answers. The net score is an estimate of total correct
answers. If the score is 100, no random answers are subtracted. If
the gross score is 33, the 33 random answers are subtracted. There-
fore: "gross score" - 100 -2*x where "x" is the "number of random
answers included as correct answers". For example, for the gross
score of 77.30, the value of "x" (rounded) is 11.35.
In 1988, a 72.8 percent of students had math scores below half the
score of "students from the high socio-economic level attending
private schools in the capital city". Of this, 58.5 percent of the
"students in private subsidized schools have similar scores".
Furthermore, in 1988, 34.9 percent of 4th grade students had a score
below 39.75 percent of the score of "students from the high socio-
economic level attending private schools in the capital city".

B. PEDAGOGICAL DECENTRALIZATION RESOURCE FLOW
1.    During the five year project's life, PRODEPE will disburse a total of
US$30 million to induce the design and implementation of about 5,000 school
quality improvement plans, according to the following schedule:
Number of Projects to be Financed
School             1992         1993        1994         1995         1996  1997  Total

Medium Risk        184          345         345          460          460   506   2,300
Low Risk            32           60          60           80           80    88      400

Cummulative

(in US$ million)

1995          6.0        17.4          20           58
1996          6.0        23.4          20           78
1997          6.6        30.0          22          100
2.    The following six weighted criteria are used to classify the municipal
and private-subsidized primary schools into high, medium, and low risk:
(a) SIMCE's (achievement scores) available time series; (b) a scale that
incorporates the number of primary education grades offered; (c) dropout and
repetition rates; (d) degree of rurality; (e) school enrollment; and (f)
socioeconomic status of student population. Primary schools, offering eight
grades or less, will be classified as high risk if they: (a) have scored low
on -recent SINCE tests; (b) have high repetition and dropout rates; and (c) are
located in low-income urban or rural areas according to CASEN and the Hinistry
of Planning (MIDEPLAN) classification (with 300 or less inhabitants, or 60 or
less dispersed households). A simulation of the ranking of all the municipal
and private subsidized schools is included in the working papers.



- 94 -
Annex 8
Page 5 of 12
3.    The yearly PRODEPE budget will be earmarked by the PIU to the 40
Educational Provinaial Departments according to a given distribution that
takes into account the following weighted normalized criteria for the
Province:
a) urban and rural primary education enrollment;
b) primary repetition and dropout rates; and
c) MIDEPLAN's extreme poverty indicators.
An illustration of such distribution follows.
4.    Once a yearly budget distribution is prepared by the PIU according to
the above methodology, the SEREMIS will be allowed to fine tune it for their
Provinces in + or - 15 percent, based in the context of the regional situation
existing at that particular time.
5.    The information regarding the modified distribution is conveyed (not yet
assigned) by the PIU to each one of the 40 Educational Provincial authorities,
for them to set an upper bound on the number of quality improvement projects
from high, medium, and low risk primary schools to be selected and approved
during that particular year.
6.    Once a given Province approves and registers its alloted annual quota of
school quality improvement projects according to the preset criteria and
procedures explained in the Operational Manual, it informs the PIU.
7.    The PIU auchorizes the transferring to the Province of up to 70 percent
of the total cost of the approved project portfolio. Resources are disbursed
according to the attached flowchart.
8.    The school enrollment criteria in conjunction with the CASEN-MIDEPLAN
urban-rural discriminator will be utilized to establish upper and lower bounds
of the funds made available for individual quality improvement projects to be
funded. The average financing will be about US$6,000 per quality improvement
project. Selected projects that could be implemented in one year will receive
up to 70 percen: of the entitled grant in one payment at the moment of the
approval, with the remaining 30 percent to be disbursed in a final payment
upon completion of project, satisfactory to the respective municipal and
provincial authorities. Selected projects requiring more than one year
implementation will receive multi-year payments totalling up to 70 percent of
the entitled grant according to a schedule specially designed to assure
project sustainability. The recipient school is obliged to contribute
additional resources erqiivalent up to 20 percent of the quality improvement
project cost, accounted in the form of teachers' time to implement the
selected project (no more than 20 percent of weekly contra-ted time), material
resources required, physical maintenance, construction, arn' :quipping.
9.    Only quality improvement projects endorsed by a minimum of four teachers
working at the same school, and supported by the school's principal, will be
considered by the supervisors and the education authorities for funding
purposes. Schools with fewer than three teachers, especially those located in
rural areas, will be encouraged tc design their quality improvement projects



- 95 -
Annex 8
Page 6 of 12
in conjunction with other schools in similar situations through appropriate
workshops involving the respective supervisor. The TS (para. 3.27) fully
supports the implementation of PRODEPE.1
10.   Before the Provincial Education authority makes the first quality
improvement project payment, agreements are signed with the school proprietor
to make him accountable for project implementation. A flowchart of such
agreement follows.
11.   An information and monitoring file  a. oppened by the Educational
Provincial authority for each quality improvement project s,nt by each
participating school (approved or not approved). Flowcharts on project
implementation monitorirg and financial control follows.
12.   Once a quality improvement project implementation is satisfactory
completed, the information is conveyed by the supervisor to the Educational
Provincial authority, and from there, to the 1"TU. The PIU then orders the
transferring of resources to the Educational Provincial authorities for them
to pay the school proprietor the outstanding balance.
13.    A quality improvement project evaluation will be implemented to analyze
PRODEPE's process and measure its impact on quality, efficiency, and equity.
1/   Article 9 defines cocurricular activities as tLose complementing classroom
teaching tasks. Article 19 provides the school institutional frame under
which teams of teachers could contribute with quality improvement projects.
Article 44 regulates the school year and establishes up to three weeks for
teacher participation in cocurricular activities.   Articles 55 and 64
stipulate that the teachers weekly time of 44 hours will comprise a maximum
of 33 hours (of 45 minutes) of in-classroom teaching activities and a
minimum of 11 hours of out-of-class cocurri-cular tasks.

Annex 11: PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION UNIT
A. Protect Management Oblective and Structure
1.    The project would be managed under the authority of the Project
Implementation Unit (PIU) directly accountable to the Minister of Education.
The PIU's main objective will be to implement all the projects components in
accordance with prescribed technical and legal requirements stipulated by the
Chilean Government and the Bank.
2.    The PIU comprises the project management unit and a matrix type
organization encompassing four substantive and three adjective divisions.'/
The substantive divisions are mainly in charge of coordinating the implemen-
tation of each one of the four project components. The three adjective
divisions are in charge of coordinating the: (a) planning, programming,
monitoring, and evaluating activities of the project; (b) procurement, civil
works, technical assistance, and personnel; and (c) financing, accounting, and
auditing (see organizational chart). The project management would be assisted
by: (i) a project Executive Council comprised by the Minister of Education or
his/her representative, the UnderSecretary of Education, the Director of
MINEDUC's Division of Planning and Budgeting, the Director of MINEDUC's
Division of Education, and the Director of the CPEIP; and (ii) a Legal
Advisor. An Advisory Council (AC) constituted by highly respected
personalities representing the education, cultural,and private sectors, as
well as political groups will assist the Minister and Under Secretary of
Education. The AC will provide guidance based on how society perceives the
effects of MECE.
B. Functions and Responsibilities
3.    The specific functions and responsibilities of the PIU and its divisions
ares
(a) Project Implementation Unit: (i) directing, coordinating, and
supervising the overall project implementation; (ii) serving as the
technical, administrative, and operational counterpart of the World Bank
during the life of the project; (iii) monitoring project execution
a2cording to the project monitoring matrix explained in Annex 13;
(iv) preparing administrative and technical guidelines and procedures
required for project implementation; (v) coordinating and administering
the bidding procedures for civil works, goods, and services required by
projects implementation; (vi) timely submitting to the Bank, disburse-
l/   In the matrix organization theory, a substantive unit is responsible of
attaining the organization's objectives (ends), whereas a unit is
responsible for providing the services (means) necessary to accomplish
the organization's goals. The performance of the substantive units
measures the effectiveness of the organization (attainment of goals),
whereas the performance of the adjective units measures the efficiencv of
the organization (resource consumption).

(vii) coordinating PIU's activities with the MINEDUC, its provincial
offices, the municipalities, decentralized agencies, and NGOs
pa=ticipating in project implementation; and (viii) periodically
reporting to the Minister of Education and the Bank on project
implementation progress.
(b) Preschool Division: (i) establishing the required plan of action to
implement the expansion of coverage and enhancement of quality of pre-
school education targeted to poor urban and rural 5-years-old;
(ii) translating, in conjunction with the PIU's Planning, Programming,
and Monitoring Division, the action plan in specific programs,
procedures, schedules, budgets, and disbursement outflows, required by
the implementation of this component; (iii) monitoring the implementa-
tion of all subcomponents contained in this project's component;
(iv) reporting to the PIU manager of any significant deviations of
project targets and procedures related to this component according to
prescribed standards; and (v) coordinating all evaluation activities
required by this component during the life of the project.
(c) Primary Education Division: (i) establishing the required plan of
acti a to implement the enhancement of the efficiency, quality, and
equity of primary education in selected schools in targeted urban and
rural low-income areas; (ii) translatine, in conjunction with the PIU's
Planning, Programming, and Monitoring Division, the action plan in
specific programs, procedures, schedulee, budgets, and disbursement
outflows, required by the implemcatatioiA of this component;
(iii) monitoring the implementation of all subcomponents contained in
this project's component; (iv) reportia:g to the PIU manager of any
significant deviations of project target: and procedures related to this
component according to prescribed standards; and (v) coordinating all
evaluation activities required by this cc_.ponent during the life of the
project.
(d) Institutional Division: (i) establishl."g the required plan of
action to implement the strengthening of the institutional capacity of
the MINEDUC, its regional and provincial offices, as well as of the
municipalities and the municipal and private subsidized preschool and
primary schools; (ii) translating, in conjunction with the PIU's
Planning, Programming, and Nonitoring Division, the action plan in
specific programs, procedures, schedules, budgets, and disbursement
outflows, required by the implementation of this component;
(iii) monitoring the implementation of all Lubcomponents contained in
this project's component; (iv) reporting to the PIU manager of any
significant deviations of project targets and procedures related to this
component according to prescribed standards; and (v) coordinating all
evaluation activities required by this component during the life of the
project



- 112 -
Annex 11
Page 4 of 7
(e) Secondary Education Division: (i) establishing the required plan of
action to assess alternative cost-eff4'ctive approaches to meet the
secondary education needs of primary education graduates; (ii) trans-
lating, in conjunction with the PIU's Planning, Programming, and
Monitoring Division, the action plan in specific programs, procedures,
schedules, budgets, and disbursement outflows, required by the implemen-
tation of this component; (iii) monitoring the implementation of all
subcomponents contained in this project's component; (iv) reporting to
the PIU manager of any significant deviations of project targets and
procedures related to this component according to prescribed standards;
and (v) coordinating all evaluation activities required by this
component during the life of the project.
(f) Planning. Programming. Monitoring, and Evaluation Division:
assisting the substantive PIU's divisions: (i) implement the action
plans established for each project component and subcomponent through
the respective programs, procedures, schedules, budgets, and disburse-
ment outflows; (ii) monitor the implementation of all components and
subcomponent of the project; and (iii) evaluate the projects component
implementation impact on quality, efficiency, and equity of education.
(g) Procurement. Civil Works. Technical Assistance. and Personnel
Division: (i) preparing the procurement documents in close coordination
with PIU's substantive divisions and in accordance with Bank specifica-
tions in each bidding procedure required by project implementation;
(ii) coordinating the bidding process, which includes, calling for bids,
evaluating bidding proposals, timely reporting to the Bank, acquiring
the required authorization from the Contraloria General de la Repablica,
signing contracts, and coordinating the executioni of specific contracts;
(iii) preparing the bidding documents in close coordination with PIU's
substantive divisions to hire technical assistance in accordance with
Bank procedures as required by project implementation; (iv) coordinating
the hiring of local and foreign consultants, which includes, calling for
consulting bids, evaluating bidding proposals, timely reporting to the
Bank, acquiring the required authorization from the Contraloria General
de la Repuiblica, signing consulting contracts, and coordinating the
execution of specific contracts; and (v) maintaining effective coordina-
tion with the PIU management and other divisions and the specialized
counterparts within the MINEDUC.
(h) Finance, Accounting. and Auditing Division: (i) disbursing funds
according to approved budget and procedures, in close coordination with
PIU's substantive divisions and management, MINEDUC's Planning and
Budgeting Division, the Ministry of Finance, and the Central Bank;
(ii) preparing the disbursement applications in accordance with Bank
procedures and guidelines; (iii) carrying out the project implementation
accounting; (Lv) promoting the auditing following the Bank's guidelines
and procedures; and (v) maintaining effective coordination with the PIU
management and other divisions and the specialized counterparts within
the MINEDUC.

4e     The specific functions and responsi1'ilities of the Project Executive
Council are: (i) providing guidance in policy and strategy; (ii) insuring the
correct application by PIU's management of targeting criteria in project
implementation; (iii) assisting the PIU management in assuring financial
sustainability during the life of the project; (iv) recommending the Minister
of Education actions to be taken concerning any conflict and dispute arising
during project implementation; and (v) analyzing the accomplishments of
project implementation targets. The Executive Council will meet regularly to
review project implementation, address policy, strategy, and organizational
problems that could affect project execution, and review project monitoring
indicators.
5.     The specific functions and responsibilities of the Legal Advisor is '.o
assist the PIU management in all legal aspects concerning project
implementation.
6.     The specific function of the Advisory Council is to provide guidance
based on how society perceives the impact of MECE. The Advisory Council will
meet twice a year.
C.  Staffing Requirements
7.     The PIU will be staffed with about:  (i) 12 full time high-caliber
professionals, with experience in their respective professional fields,
preferably with background in project implementation of similar nature, 3
technical assistants, and 6 support staff. The personnel distribution among
all the PIU's units is shown below.

D.  Contents of the Agreements Between MINEDUC and Imilementing Agencies
9.     Objectives:  (i) to implement a pedagogical decentrali7ation program
(PRODEPE) in the municipal and private-subsidized primary schools by
establishing locally-based quality improvement projects.
Responsibilities and ObliRations of the Municipalities or Schools
Proprietors:
(a)  prepares and submits to MIN4EDUC (Provincial Secretariat) quality
improvement projects;
(b) implements the projects approved by the Provincial Secretariat;
(c) keeps separate accounts for the expenditures incurred under the
agreement, and follows the accounting and auditing procedures
satisfactory to the Bank; and
(d) allows PIU and Bank staff to supervise the execution of the signed
agreement, including accessing to the physical facilities and the
accounting system.
10.    Objectives:  (i) to construct additional classrooms in incomplete rural
schools and; (ii) to upgrade and remodel primary schools.
Responsibilities and Obligations of the Municipalities:
(a) selects the primary schools to be benefitted with the construction
of additional classrooms and remodeling of its facilities, and submits
these selections to MINEDUC for approval (Provincial Secretariat);
(b)  procures the construction of classrooms using Bank's guidelines
and procedures;
(c) keeps separate accounts for the expenditures incurred under the
agreement, and follows the accounting and auditing procedures
satisfactory to the Bank; and
(d) allows PIU and Bank staff to supervise the execution of the signed
agreement, including accessing to the physical facilities and the
accounting system.
11.    Objectives:  (i) to provide didactic material; (ii) to hire additional
teachers; (iii) to expand the school feeding program; and (iv) to construct
additional classrooms.
Responsibilities and Obligations of the Municipalities:
(a) selects the preschools to be benefitted with didactic material,
hiring additional teachers, feeding program and construction of
additional classrooms, and submits these selections to MINEDUC for
approval (Provincial Secretariat);
(b) distributes the didactic material to the selected preschools;
(c) pays teachers salaries according to the provision of the Teachers'
Statute;

Annex 11
Page 7 of 7
(d) procures the construction of classrooms using applicable
procurement procedures;
(e) keeps separate accounts for the expenditures incurred under the
agreement, and follows the accounting and auditing procedures
satisfactory to the Bank; and
(f) allows PIU and Bank staff to supervise the execution of the signed
agreement, including accessing to the physical facilities and the
accounting system.
12.    Objectives:  (i) to provide didactic material; (ii) to hire supervisors
and paraprofessionals; (iii) to expand the preschool feeding program; and (iv)
to refurbish and equip small rural centers (INTEGRA).
Responsibilities and Obligations of Municipalities. INTEGRA and JUNJI:
(a) selects the preschools to be benefitted with didactic material,
hiring additional teachers, feeding program and construction of
additional classrooms, and submits these selections to PIU/MINEDUC for
approval;
(b) procures and distributes the didactic material to the selected
preschools;
(c) pays teachers salaries according to the provision of the Teachers'
Statute;
(d) procures the refurbishing and equipping small rural centers
(INTEGRA) following applicable procurement procedures;
(e) keeps separate accounts for the expenditures incurred under the
agreement, and follows the accounting and auditing procedures
satisfactory to the Bank; and
(f) allows PIU and Bank staff to supervise the execution of the signed
agreement, including accessing to the physical facilities and the
accounting system.
13.    Responsibilities of MINEDUC:
(a) transfers loan proceeds as required as a grant;
(b) disseminates the procedures and targeting criteria to select
beneficiaries;
(c) procures, stores and distributes goods and services as required
following applicable procurement procedures;
(d) supervises the implementation of the different agreements and
subprojects; and
(e) provides training to implementing agencies and muncipal officials.

Annex 13: PROJECT MONITORING MATRIX AND SUPERVISION ARRANGEMENTS
A. KEY PROJECT PERFORMANCE INDICATORS
11.e key monitoring indicators to track the project's performance are
listed in the matrix below (Annex 13, Table 1). It is unrealistic in the
education sectors worldwide to set short- and medium-term targets on
efficiency, equity and quality, due to the very slow, and sometimes
unpredictable response of the educational systems to change. However, it is
advisable to monitor the change of the proposed indicators over time,
expecting improvements when compared with the corresponding base information
(current values).
